Question 4
Question ID: creational-factory-method:B4
Paired Question ID: creational-factory-method:A4
Competency ID: fm-identify
Bloom Level: Analyze
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Examine:  struct ShapeCreator { virtual Shape* make() = 0; };  struct CircleCreator : ShapeCreator { Shape* make() override { return new Circle(); } };  Which pattern is this structural evidence of?

OPTIONS:
A. Factory Method
B. Builder
C. Prototype
D. Abstract Factory

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Factory Method

RATIONALE:
A virtual make() overridden by a subclass to return one concrete product is Factory Method; it produces a single product, not a family or a step-built object.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* Nesteruk (2022), Design Patterns in Modern C++

Difficulty: Difficult

QUESTION:
A reviewer sees a creator base class with create() overridden by two subclasses to return different products. Is calling this Factory Method defensible?

OPTIONS:
A. No — only a string switch counts as Factory Method
B. No — it must also be a Singleton
C. Yes — an overridable creation method specialised by subclasses to choose the concrete product is exactly Factory Method
D. Yes — but only if the class is named Factory

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. Yes — an overridable creation method specialised by subclasses to choose the concrete product is exactly Factory Method

RATIONALE:
Subclasses overriding a creation method to pick the concrete product is the defining structure of Factory Method; the name and Singleton status are irrelevant.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

QUESTION FORMAT: FOUR-OPTION MCQ

QUESTION:
In Factory Method, which participant decides which concrete product is actually created?

OPTIONS:
A. The client code, by naming the concrete class directly
B. A subclass that overrides the creation method
C. A global registry shared by all callers
D. The destructor of the base product

CORRECT ANSWER: B. A subclass that overrides the creation method

RATIONALE:
The base creator declares a creation method; a subclass overrides it to choose the concrete product. The other options bypass the subclass-driven choice that defines the pattern.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Creational module content
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

Question 2
Question ID: creational-factory-method:understanding
Assessment Type: In-Module Conceptual Assessment
Source Question Index: 1
Competency ID: fm-understand
Bloom Level: Understanding
Applicable: YES
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft
QUESTION FORMAT: FOUR-OPTION MCQ

QUESTION:
How does Factory Method differ from a factory that produces a whole matching set of related objects?

OPTIONS:
A. It cannot use inheritance at all
B. It always returns a single shared instance
C. It is identical — both create families
D. It defers the choice of one product to a subclass override, rather than creating a coordinated family of products

CORRECT ANSWER: D. It defers the choice of one product to a subclass override, rather than creating a coordinated family of products

RATIONALE:
Factory Method picks one product through a subclass-overridden method; producing a coordinated family of related products is Abstract Factory.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Creational module content
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

QUESTION:
A report engine runs an identical generate-and-export sequence, but each report kind must construct its own exporter object. The base class wants to stay unaware of which concrete exporter is used. Which approach fits?

OPTIONS:
A. Singleton
B. Prototype
C. Factory Method
D. Method Chaining

CORRECT ANSWER: C. Factory Method

RATIONALE:
A fixed flow whose single sub-object is chosen by subclasses through an overridable creation method is Factory Method; the distractors solve single-instance, copying, and fluent configuration.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

QUESTION:
Examine:  struct Parser { virtual Token* lex() = 0; void run(){ Token* t = lex(); /* ... */ } };  struct JsonParser : Parser { Token* lex() override { return new JsonToken(); } };  Which pattern does this structure indicate?

OPTIONS:
A. Factory Method
B. Abstract Factory
C. Builder
D. Prototype

CORRECT ANSWER: A. Factory Method

RATIONALE:
A base class calls its own overridable creation method while a subclass implements it to return one concrete product — Factory Method. There is no family, copy, or stepwise build.

CATEGORY: CREATIONAL

MODULE ID: creational-method-chaining
ASSESSMENT TYPE: Formal Pre-Test — Form A
APPLICABLE BLOOM LEVELS: Remember, Understand, Apply, Analyze, Evaluate

================================================================Question 1
Question ID: creational-method-chaining:A1
Paired Question ID: creational-method-chaining:B1
Competency ID: mc-intent
Bloom Level: Remember
Difficulty: Easy
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
What does the Method Chaining idiom let you do?

OPTIONS:
A. Set several options on the same object in one fluent expression
B. Guarantee one instance
C. Create matching product families
D. Convert an incompatible interface

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Set several options on the same object in one fluent expression

RATIONALE:
Method Chaining returns the same object from each setter so calls can be chained fluently. The distractors are Singleton, Abstract Factory, and Adapter.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Nesteruk (2022), Design Patterns in Modern C++
* CodiNeo Creational module content

------------------------------------------------
Question 2
Question ID: creational-method-chaining:A2
Paired Question ID: creational-method-chaining:B2
Competency ID: mc-understand
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
What must a setter return to enable method chaining in C++?

OPTIONS:
A. void
B. a new copy of the object
C. a reference (or pointer) to the same object, e.g. return *this
D. a boolean success flag

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. a reference (or pointer) to the same object, e.g. return *this

RATIONALE:
Returning *this (a reference to the same object) lets the next call chain on it. Returning void, a copy, or a flag breaks the chain.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Nesteruk (2022), Design Patterns in Modern C++

------------------------------------------------
Question 3
Question ID: creational-method-chaining:A3
Paired Question ID: creational-method-chaining:B3
Competency ID: mc-select
Bloom Level: Apply
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A configuration object should be set up in one readable expression like cfg.host("x").port(80).tls(true);. Which technique enables this?

OPTIONS:
A. Returning void from each setter
B. Making the class a Singleton
C. Cloning the object per setter
D. Having each setter return a reference to the object so calls chain

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. Having each setter return a reference to the object so calls chain

RATIONALE:
Each setter returning a self-reference makes the fluent chain possible. The other options prevent chaining or solve unrelated problems.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Nesteruk (2022), Design Patterns in Modern C++

------------------------------------------------
Question 4
Question ID: creational-method-chaining:A4
Paired Question ID: creational-method-chaining:B4
Competency ID: mc-identify
Bloom Level: Analyze
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Examine:  struct Pizza { Pizza& size(int s){ s_=s; return *this; } Pizza& cheese(bool c){ c_=c; return *this; } };  What idiom does this show?

OPTIONS:
A. Method Chaining (fluent interface)
B. Prototype
C. Singleton
D. Abstract Factory

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Method Chaining (fluent interface)

RATIONALE:
Setters that mutate and return *this form a fluent interface (Method Chaining). No copying, single-instance, or family creation is present.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Nesteruk (2022), Design Patterns in Modern C++

------------------------------------------------
Question 5
Question ID: creational-method-chaining:A5
Paired Question ID: creational-method-chaining:B5
Competency ID: mc-evaluate
Bloom Level: Evaluate
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A “fluent” API returns a brand-new copy of the object from every setter instead of *this. Does this correctly implement method chaining, and what is the effect?

OPTIONS:
A. Yes — it is identical to returning *this
B. Yes — copies are required for chaining
C. No — chaining is impossible in C++
D. No — returning copies means earlier settings may be lost and each call mutates a different object, defeating the fluent intent

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. No — returning copies means earlier settings may be lost and each call mutates a different object, defeating the fluent intent

RATIONALE:
Returning fresh copies means each call operates on a different object, so accumulated configuration can be lost; chaining requires returning the same object (*this).

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Nesteruk (2022), Design Patterns in Modern C++

Question 2
Question ID: creational-method-chaining:B2
Paired Question ID: creational-method-chaining:A2
Competency ID: mc-understand

Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Why does each chained method return *this?

OPTIONS:
A. To make a defensive copy
B. To enforce a single instance
C. To convert the return type
D. So the following call can operate on the same object, continuing the chain

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. So the following call can operate on the same object, continuing the chain

RATIONALE:
Returning *this hands the same object to the next call, sustaining the fluent chain. The other options are unrelated.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Nesteruk (2022), Design Patterns in Modern C++

------------------------------------------------
Question 3
Question ID: creational-method-chaining:B3
Paired Question ID: creational-method-chaining:A3
Competency ID: mc-select
Bloom Level: Apply
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A query builder should read like q.select("*").from("t").where("id=1");. What enables this fluent style?

OPTIONS:
A. Each method returns void
B. Each method returns a reference to the builder so calls chain
C. The builder is cloned each call
D. The builder is a Singleton

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. Each method returns a reference to the builder so calls chain

RATIONALE:
Each method returning a reference to the builder enables chaining; void, cloning, or Singleton would not.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Nesteruk (2022), Design Patterns in Modern C++

------------------------------------------------
Question 4
Question ID: creational-method-chaining:B4
Paired Question ID: creational-method-chaining:A4
Competency ID: mc-identify
Bloom Level: Analyze
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Examine:  Query& Query::where(std::string c){ clauses_.push_back(c); return *this; }  // similar for select(), from()  Which idiom is this?

OPTIONS:
A. Method Chaining (fluent interface)
B. Singleton
C. Prototype
D. Factory Method

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Method Chaining (fluent interface)

RATIONALE:
Mutating member functions that return *this form a fluent (method-chaining) interface; there is no single-instance, copy, or factory structure.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Nesteruk (2022), Design Patterns in Modern C++

------------------------------------------------
Question 5
Question ID: creational-method-chaining:B5
Paired Question ID: creational-method-chaining:A5
Competency ID: mc-evaluate
Bloom Level: Evaluate
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A setter in a “fluent” class returns void but the author still writes a.x().y();. Is the chaining implemented correctly?

OPTIONS:
A. Yes — void return is fine for chaining
B. Yes — provided the methods are const
C. No — with a void return the second call has no object to act on, so it will not compile/chain
D. No — only Singletons can chain

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. No — with a void return the second call has no object to act on, so it will not compile/chain

RATIONALE:
Chaining needs each call to yield the object; a void return leaves nothing to call the next method on, so the chain cannot work.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Nesteruk (2022), Design Patterns in Modern C++

What does the Method Chaining idiom let you do in a single expression?

OPTIONS:
A. Set several options on the same object one after another
B. Guarantee that only one instance of the object exists

D. Clone a pre-configured object

RATIONALE:
Method Chaining returns the same object from each call so options can be set in one fluent expression. The distractors are Singleton, Abstract Factory, and Prototype.

QUESTION FORMAT: FOUR-OPTION MCQ

QUESTION:
Method Chaining and Builder both rely on returning *this. What distinguishes Method Chaining from Builder?

OPTIONS:
A. Method Chaining must be a Singleton
B. Method Chaining clones the object on each call
C. Method Chaining configures the same object fluently and has no separate terminal step that hands back a distinct finished product
D. Method Chaining can only set one option total

CORRECT ANSWER: C. Method Chaining configures the same object fluently and has no separate terminal step that hands back a distinct finished product

RATIONALE:
Both share the return-*this shape, but Method Chaining simply configures one object fluently, whereas Builder adds a terminal step yielding a separate finished product.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Nesteruk (2022), Design Patterns in Modern C++
* CodiNeo Creational module content

QUESTION:
A developer wants to write log.level(Warn).tag("net").color(true); on one line, mutating the same logger and reading like a sentence, with no separate finished object produced. Which technique enables this?

OPTIONS:
A. Make the logger a Singleton
B. Have each setter return a reference to the same logger so calls chain
C. Return a fresh copy of the logger from each setter
D. Provide one interface that creates a family of loggers

CORRECT ANSWER: B. Have each setter return a reference to the same logger so calls chain

RATIONALE:
Each setter returning a reference to the same object enables the fluent chain on one logger; the distractors prevent chaining or solve unrelated problems.

* Nesteruk (2022), Design Patterns in Modern C++

Question 4

QUESTION:
Examine:  struct Style { Style& bold(bool b){ b_=b; return *this; } Style& size(int s){ s_=s; return *this; } };  with no terminal method returning a different type. Which idiom does this structure indicate?

OPTIONS:
A. Factory Method
B. Prototype
C. Abstract Factory
D. Method Chaining (a fluent interface)

CORRECT ANSWER: D. Method Chaining (a fluent interface)

RATIONALE:
Mutating setters that return *this with no terminal step producing a distinct product form a fluent interface (Method Chaining). The absence of a build()-style terminal distinguishes it from Builder.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Nesteruk (2022), Design Patterns in Modern C++

Question 5
Question ID: creational-method-chaining:evaluating
Assessment Type: In-Module Conceptual Assessment
Source Question Index: 4
Competency ID: mc-evaluate
Bloom Level: Evaluating
Applicable: YES
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft
QUESTION FORMAT: FOUR-OPTION MCQ

QUESTION:
A "fluent" class declares its setters to return a brand-new copy of the object instead of *this. Does this implement method chaining correctly, and what is the effect?

OPTIONS:
A. No — each call mutates a different copy, so accumulated settings can be lost and the chain no longer configures one object
B. Yes — returning copies is required for chaining
C. Yes — it behaves identically to returning *this
D. No — chaining is impossible in C++ regardless

CORRECT ANSWER: A. No — each call mutates a different copy, so accumulated settings can be lost and the chain no longer configures one object

RATIONALE:
Returning a fresh copy each call means each setter acts on a different object, so earlier settings may be discarded; correct chaining returns *this so the same object is configured.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Nesteruk (2022), Design Patterns in Modern C++

================================================================Question 1
Question ID: creational-prototype:A1
Paired Question ID: creational-prototype:B1
Competency ID: pr-intent
Bloom Level: Remember
Difficulty: Easy
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
What is the intent of the Prototype pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. Create new objects by copying an existing instance (a prototype)
B. Guarantee a single instance
C. Let subclasses choose the concrete product
D. Assemble an object through ordered steps

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Create new objects by copying an existing instance (a prototype)

RATIONALE:
Prototype creates new objects by cloning an existing prototypical instance. The distractors are Singleton, Factory Method, and Builder.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Creational module content

------------------------------------------------
Question 2
Question ID: creational-prototype:A2
Paired Question ID: creational-prototype:B2
Competency ID: pr-understand
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Why is Prototype useful when object creation is expensive or configuration-heavy?

OPTIONS:
A. It avoids creating objects at all
B. It copies a ready, pre-configured instance instead of rebuilding from scratch
C. It guarantees thread safety
D. It removes the need for constructors

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. It copies a ready, pre-configured instance instead of rebuilding from scratch

RATIONALE:
Cloning a pre-configured prototype reuses its setup, avoiding costly re-construction. The other options misstate Prototype’s benefit.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 3
Question ID: creational-prototype:A3
Paired Question ID: creational-prototype:B3
Competency ID: pr-select
Bloom Level: Apply
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
An editor must duplicate a fully-configured shape (with styling) at runtime without knowing its concrete type. Which pattern fits?

OPTIONS:
A. Singleton
B. Abstract Factory
C. Method Chaining
D. Prototype

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. Prototype

RATIONALE:
Duplicating a configured object polymorphically (without its concrete type) is Prototype via clone(); the distractors solve other problems.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

Question 4
Question ID: creational-prototype:A4
Paired Question ID: creational-prototype:B4
Competency ID: pr-identify
Bloom Level: Analyze
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Examine:  struct Shape { virtual Shape* clone() const = 0; };  struct Circle : Shape { Shape* clone() const override { return new Circle(*this); } };  Which pattern is this?

OPTIONS:
A. Prototype
B. Factory Method
C. Builder
D. Singleton

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Prototype

RATIONALE:
A virtual clone() that copy-constructs the same concrete type is the Prototype structure; it copies rather than choosing or assembling a product.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* Nesteruk (2022), Design Patterns in Modern C++

------------------------------------------------
Question 5
Question ID: creational-prototype:A5
Paired Question ID: creational-prototype:B5
Competency ID: pr-evaluate
Bloom Level: Evaluate
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A clone() returns new Base(*this) for every subclass (no override), so cloning a Derived yields a Base. Is this a correct Prototype, and why?

OPTIONS:
A. Yes — base copying is sufficient
B. Yes — provided Base is abstract
C. No — Prototype forbids inheritance
D. No — without a polymorphic override clone() slices the object, losing the derived type

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. No — without a polymorphic override clone() slices the object, losing the derived type

RATIONALE:
Cloning must be polymorphic so each subclass copies its own type; a fixed new Base(*this) slices derived objects, breaking Prototype.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Nesteruk (2022), Design Patterns in Modern C++

B. Abstract Factory
C. Prototype
D. Singleton

C. Prototype

RATIONALE:
A polymorphic clone() that returns a copy of the concrete subclass is the Prototype pattern; no stepwise build or family creation is involved.

* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

Question 5
Question ID: creational-prototype:B5
Paired Question ID: creational-prototype:A5
Competency ID: pr-evaluate
Bloom Level: Evaluate
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A Prototype holds a raw pointer member and clone() does a shallow copy. What is the risk, and is it a sound clone?

OPTIONS:
A. No risk — shallow copy is always correct
B. Sound only if the class is final
C. Unsound — Prototype cannot hold pointers
D. Risky/unsound — both objects share the same pointed-to resource, so a deep copy is usually needed for an independent clone

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. Risky/unsound — both objects share the same pointed-to resource, so a deep copy is usually needed for an independent clone

RATIONALE:
A shallow copy makes clones share the same owned resource, causing aliasing/double-free bugs; an independent clone usually needs a deep copy.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Nesteruk (2022), Design Patterns in Modern C++

What is the core idea of the Prototype pattern?

QUESTION:
Why does cloning rely on a virtual clone() rather than letting the caller write new ConcreteType(original)?

OPTIONS:
A. So the caller can copy an object through a base pointer without knowing or naming its concrete type
B. Because virtual methods are faster than constructors
C. Because Prototype objects may not have constructors
D. Because clone() guarantees the object becomes a Singleton

CORRECT ANSWER: A. So the caller can copy an object through a base pointer without knowing or naming its concrete type

RATIONALE:
A polymorphic clone() lets callers copy through the base interface without knowing the concrete type, which is the whole point of Prototype; naming the concrete type would defeat it.

Validation Status: draft
QUESTION FORMAT: FOUR-OPTION MCQ

QUESTION:
A Prototype subclass owns a pointer to a heap buffer, and its clone() performs only a member-wise (shallow) copy. Is this a sound clone, and why?

OPTIONS:
A. Sound — shallow copies are always correct for Prototype
B. Sound only if the class is marked final
C. Unsound — both objects end up sharing the same buffer, so a deep copy is needed for a truly independent clone
D. Unsound — Prototype classes may not own pointers

CORRECT ANSWER: C. Unsound — both objects end up sharing the same buffer, so a deep copy is needed for a truly independent clone

RATIONALE:
A shallow copy makes the clone and original share the same owned buffer, risking aliasing and double-free; an independent clone usually requires a deep copy of owned resources.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Nesteruk (2022), Design Patterns in Modern C++

================================================================Question 1
Question ID: creational-singleton:A1
Paired Question ID: creational-singleton:B1
Competency ID: singleton-intent
Bloom Level: Remember
Difficulty: Easy
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
What does the Singleton pattern guarantee?

OPTIONS:
A. A class has exactly one instance with a single global access point
B. A class can be freely subclassed and copied
C. Objects are produced in matching families
D. Objects are built through ordered steps

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. A class has exactly one instance with a single global access point

RATIONALE:
Singleton ensures a class has exactly one instance and provides one global access point to it. The other options describe subclassing, Abstract Factory, and Builder.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Creational module content

------------------------------------------------
Question 2
Question ID: creational-singleton:A2
Paired Question ID: creational-singleton:B2
Competency ID: singleton-enforce
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
How is the single-instance guarantee typically enforced in C++?

OPTIONS:
A. By marking every method const
B. By making the class abstract
C. By a non-public constructor plus a static accessor that returns the sole instance
D. By deleting the destructor

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. By a non-public constructor plus a static accessor that returns the sole instance

RATIONALE:
A hidden (non-public) constructor prevents outside construction, and a static accessor returns the one instance. The other options do not constrain instance count.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Nesteruk (2022), Design Patterns in Modern C++
* CodiNeo Creational module content

------------------------------------------------
Question 3
Question ID: creational-singleton:A3
Paired Question ID: creational-singleton:B3
Competency ID: singleton-select
Bloom Level: Apply
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A system needs exactly one shared configuration object, created once and reachable everywhere. Which pattern fits?

OPTIONS:
A. Prototype
B. Factory Method
C. Abstract Factory
D. Singleton

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. Singleton

RATIONALE:
One shared instance with a global access point is the Singleton use case; the distractors address copying, deferred subclass choice, and product families.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 4

Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Examine:  class Cfg { Cfg() = default; public: static Cfg& instance() { static Cfg c; return c; } };  Which pattern does this indicate?

OPTIONS:
A. Singleton
B. Factory Method
C. Builder
D. Prototype

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Singleton

RATIONALE:
A non-public constructor plus a static accessor returning one function-local static is the (Meyers) Singleton idiom; nothing here creates families, products, or copies.

* Nesteruk (2022), Design Patterns in Modern C++

QUESTION:
A class exposes a public static getInstance() but also leaves its constructor public. Does this correctly implement Singleton, and why?

OPTIONS:
A. Yes — the static getter is sufficient
B. Yes — provided the class is marked final
C. No — Singletons must allocate on the heap
D. No — a public constructor lets callers create extra instances, breaking the single-instance guarantee

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. No — a public constructor lets callers create extra instances, breaking the single-instance guarantee

RATIONALE:
The single-instance invariant requires preventing outside construction; a public constructor allows additional instances, so the implementation is incorrect regardless of the getter.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* Nesteruk (2022), Design Patterns in Modern C++

QUESTION:
The Singleton pattern primarily ensures that ___.

OPTIONS:
A. objects are cloned from a prototype
B. related product families are produced together
C. one instance is reachable from a single global access point
D. an object is assembled step by step

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. one instance is reachable from a single global access point

RATIONALE:
Singleton ensures a single instance with one global access point. The distractors describe Prototype, Abstract Factory, and Builder.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Creational module content

Question 2
Question ID: creational-singleton:B2
Paired Question ID: creational-singleton:A2
Competency ID: singleton-enforce
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Which member combination is characteristic of a C++ Singleton?

OPTIONS:
A. A public constructor and a virtual destructor
B. Several overloaded public constructors
C. A public clone() method
D. A hidden constructor and a static method returning the sole instance

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. A hidden constructor and a static method returning the sole instance

RATIONALE:
A hidden constructor blocks external construction while a static accessor returns the one instance. Public/overloaded constructors permit many instances; clone() is Prototype.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Nesteruk (2022), Design Patterns in Modern C++
* CodiNeo Creational module content

------------------------------------------------
Question 3
Question ID: creational-singleton:B3
Paired Question ID: creational-singleton:A3
Competency ID: singleton-select
Bloom Level: Apply
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
An application must route all logging through one logger object, created on first use and shared app-wide. Which pattern best fits?

OPTIONS:
A. Builder
B. Singleton
C. Prototype
D. Adapter

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. Singleton

RATIONALE:
A single shared, lazily-created object with global access is Singleton; the distractors solve construction, copying, and interface conversion.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

Question 4
Question ID: creational-singleton:B4
Paired Question ID: creational-singleton:A4
Competency ID: singleton-identify
Bloom Level: Analyze
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Examine:  // ctor is private
  Logger& logger() { static Logger inst; return inst; }  Which pattern is this idiom?

OPTIONS:
A. Singleton
B. Prototype
C. Abstract Factory
D. Method Chaining

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Singleton

RATIONALE:
A private constructor plus a function-local static returned by reference is the lazy (Meyers) Singleton; it is not copying, family creation, or fluent chaining.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Nesteruk (2022), Design Patterns in Modern C++

Question 5
Question ID: creational-singleton:B5
Paired Question ID: creational-singleton:A5
Competency ID: singleton-evaluate
Bloom Level: Evaluate
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A developer keeps the instance in a global pointer and calls new in several places “just in case.” Is this a sound Singleton?

OPTIONS:
A. Yes — a global pointer guarantees uniqueness
B. Yes — provided it is wrapped in a namespace
C. No — multiple new calls create multiple instances, violating the single-instance invariant
D. No — Singletons cannot use pointers at all

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. No — multiple new calls create multiple instances, violating the single-instance invariant

RATIONALE:
Calling new in several places creates several instances, breaking uniqueness; the access mechanism (global pointer/namespace) does not enforce a single instance.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Nesteruk (2022), Design Patterns in Modern C++

QUESTION:
In the kitchen-phone analogy from the module, what does Singleton ultimately provide?

OPTIONS:
A. A way to assemble one object part by part
B. A way to copy a configured object cheaply
C. Exactly one shared object reached through a single access point
D. A family of matching objects created together

CORRECT ANSWER: C. Exactly one shared object reached through a single access point

RATIONALE:
Like the one kitchen phone everyone shares, Singleton yields exactly one instance reached through a single access point. The distractors describe Builder, Prototype, and Abstract Factory.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Creational module content
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

Question 2
Question ID: creational-singleton:understanding
Assessment Type: In-Module Conceptual Assessment
Source Question Index: 1
Competency ID: singleton-enforce
Bloom Level: Understanding
Applicable: YES
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft
QUESTION FORMAT: FOUR-OPTION MCQ

QUESTION:
Why does hiding the constructor matter for a Singleton, and how is it different from simply storing an object in a global variable?

OPTIONS:
A. Because hiding the constructor stops outside code from constructing extra instances, whereas a plain global still lets anyone call the constructor again
B. Because a global variable cannot be accessed from other files
C. Because Singletons are forbidden from using static storage
D. Because hiding the constructor makes the object copyable by default

CORRECT ANSWER: A. Because hiding the constructor stops outside code from constructing extra instances, whereas a plain global still lets anyone call the constructor again

RATIONALE:
A non-public constructor is what enforces uniqueness; a plain global object does not prevent additional construction, so the single-instance invariant is not guaranteed.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Nesteruk (2022), Design Patterns in Modern C++
* CodiNeo Creational module content

QUESTION:
A game engine needs one audio mixer: created the first time sound plays, then reused by every system that emits a sound, with no second mixer ever existing. Which approach matches?

OPTIONS:
A. Prototype
B. Builder
C. Abstract Factory
D. Singleton

CORRECT ANSWER: D. Singleton

RATIONALE:
One lazily-created object shared everywhere with no duplicates is the Singleton use case; the distractors solve copying, stepwise construction, and family creation.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Creational module content
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

QUESTION:
Examine:  class Clock { Clock(){} public: static Clock& get(){ static Clock c; return c; } long now(); };  Which pattern does this structure indicate?

OPTIONS:
A. Factory Method
B. Singleton (Meyers function-local static)
C. Prototype
D. Method Chaining

CORRECT ANSWER: B. Singleton (Meyers function-local static)

RATIONALE:
A non-public constructor plus a static accessor returning a single function-local static is the Meyers Singleton idiom; nothing here copies, chains, or chooses a product.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Nesteruk (2022), Design Patterns in Modern C++

Question 5
Question ID: creational-singleton:evaluating
Assessment Type: In-Module Conceptual Assessment
Source Question Index: 4

Applicable: YES
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft
QUESTION FORMAT: FOUR-OPTION MCQ

QUESTION:
A class offers a static current() accessor but its copy constructor is still public, so callers can write Settings s2 = Settings::current();. Does this faithfully implement Singleton?

OPTIONS:
A. Yes — a static accessor is all that is required
B. Yes — copying is encouraged so each caller gets its own settings
C. No — a public copy constructor lets callers duplicate the instance, breaking the single-instance guarantee
D. No — Singletons must be allocated with new

CORRECT ANSWER: C. No — a public copy constructor lets callers duplicate the instance, breaking the single-instance guarantee

RATIONALE:
Uniqueness must be protected against copying too; a public copy constructor lets callers clone the instance, violating the invariant regardless of the accessor.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* Nesteruk (2022), Design Patterns in Modern C++

================================================================Question 1
Question ID: structural-adapter:A1
Paired Question ID: structural-adapter:B1
Competency ID: ad-intent
Bloom Level: Remember
Difficulty: Easy
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
What is the intent of the Adapter pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. Convert a class’s interface into another interface clients expect, so incompatible types can work together
B. Add new behaviour to an object by wrapping it
C. Control or restrict access to an object
D. Ensure a class has only one instance

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Convert a class’s interface into another interface clients expect, so incompatible types can work together

RATIONALE:
Adapter converts one existing interface into the interface a client expects. The distractors describe Decorator, Proxy, and Singleton.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Structural module content

------------------------------------------------
Question 2
Question ID: structural-adapter:A2
Paired Question ID: structural-adapter:B2
Competency ID: ad-mechanism
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
How does an Adapter make two incompatible interfaces work together?

OPTIONS:
A. It edits the adaptee’s source code to match the client
B. It implements the target interface and translates each call to the adaptee’s existing methods
C. It duplicates the adaptee for each client
D. It deletes the target interface entirely

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. It implements the target interface and translates each call to the adaptee’s existing methods

RATIONALE:
The adapter conforms to the target interface and forwards/translates calls to the unchanged adaptee. It does not modify or copy the adaptee.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 3
Question ID: structural-adapter:A3
Paired Question ID: structural-adapter:B3
Competency ID: ad-select
Bloom Level: Apply
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A new reporting client expects a JSON-exporter interface, but the only library available exposes a completely different XML API you cannot change. Which pattern lets them work together?

OPTIONS:
A. Decorator
B. Proxy
C. Adapter
D. Composite

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. Adapter

RATIONALE:
Bridging a required interface to an unchangeable, differently-shaped existing one is Adapter. The distractors add behaviour, control access, or build trees.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

Examine:  class JsonTarget { public: virtual std::string toJson() = 0; };  class XmlLib { public: std::string toXml(); };  class XmlToJson : public JsonTarget { XmlLib lib; public: std::string toJson() override { return convert(lib.toXml()); } };  Which pattern is this?

OPTIONS:
A. Bridge
B. Facade
C. Proxy
D. Adapter

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. Adapter

RATIONALE:
A class implementing the expected interface (JsonTarget) by translating to an existing, differently-shaped API (XmlLib) is Adapter.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A class claims to be an Adapter but simply adds caching around the very same interface the client already used, converting nothing. Is this correctly an Adapter?

OPTIONS:
A. No — Adapter must convert one interface into another; adding behaviour over the same interface is Decorator/Proxy, not Adapter
B. Yes — any wrapper is an Adapter
C. Yes — caching counts as adapting
D. No — Adapters cannot use composition

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. No — Adapter must convert one interface into another; adding behaviour over the same interface is Decorator/Proxy, not Adapter

RATIONALE:
Adapter’s defining purpose is interface conversion. Adding behaviour while keeping the same interface is Decorator/Proxy intent, not Adapter.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Structural module content

QUESTION:
The Adapter pattern is best described as a way to ___.

OPTIONS:
A. guarantee a single shared instance
B. make one existing interface usable through a different, expected interface
C. share intrinsic state across many objects
D. pass a request along a chain of handlers

QUESTION:
What is the adapter’s role relative to the adaptee and the client?

OPTIONS:
A. It changes the client to match the adaptee
B. It merges client and adaptee into one class
C. It conforms to the interface the client expects and delegates work to the adaptee
D. It hides the adaptee permanently from all code

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. It conforms to the interface the client expects and delegates work to the adaptee

RATIONALE:
The adapter presents the client-expected interface and delegates to the adaptee, leaving both client and adaptee unchanged.

* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

QUESTION:
When an adapter receives a call, what does it actually do with the wrapped (adaptee) object?

OPTIONS:
A. It rewrites the adaptee so the two interfaces become identical
B. It forwards the call to the adaptee, translating names and argument types as needed
C. It stores the adaptee and discards every incoming call
D. It copies the adaptee once per caller to avoid sharing

CORRECT ANSWER: B. It forwards the call to the adaptee, translating names and argument types as needed

RATIONALE:
The adapter conforms to the expected interface and delegates each call to the unchanged adaptee, translating signatures. It never edits or duplicates the adaptee.

Question 3
Question ID: structural-adapter:applying

QUESTION:
Your analytics layer is written against a Geocoder type with lookup(Address), but the only mapping vendor you can use ships a frozen SDK whose method is resolve(const char*, double&, double&). You may not edit the SDK. What design lets your layer call it unchanged?

OPTIONS:
A. A subsystem front that hides the SDK behind one easy method
B. A wrapper that adds caching to the SDK while keeping its interface
C. A tree of vendor objects treated uniformly
D. A class implementing Geocoder that translates each lookup into the SDK’s resolve call

CORRECT ANSWER: D. A class implementing Geocoder that translates each lookup into the SDK’s resolve call

RATIONALE:
A class that presents the expected Geocoder interface while converting calls to the differently-shaped, unmodifiable SDK is an Adapter. The distractors are Facade, Decorator, and Composite.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Structural module content
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 4
Question ID: structural-adapter:analyzing
Assessment Type: In-Module Conceptual Assessment
Source Question Index: 3
Competency ID: adapter-identify
Bloom Level: Analyzing
Applicable: YES
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft
QUESTION FORMAT: FOUR-OPTION MCQ

QUESTION:
Examine:

struct TempSensor { virtual double celsius() = 0; };
struct VendorProbe { double readKelvin(); };
struct ProbeBridge : TempSensor {
  VendorProbe p;
  double celsius() override { return p.readKelvin() - 273.15; }
};

Which structural pattern does ProbeBridge implement?

OPTIONS:
A. Bridge, because the name says so and it holds another object
B. Decorator, because it changes the returned value
C. Adapter, because it implements the expected interface by converting calls to a differently-shaped existing API
D. Proxy, because it stands in for the probe

CORRECT ANSWER: C. Adapter, because it implements the expected interface by converting calls to a differently-shaped existing API

RATIONALE:
Despite its name, ProbeBridge implements the expected TempSensor interface and converts a Kelvin reading into the expected Celsius call — interface conversion, i.e. Adapter. It does not vary two hierarchies (Bridge), add client-facing features (Decorator), or control access (Proxy).

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Structural module content
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 5
Question ID: structural-adapter:evaluating
Assessment Type: In-Module Conceptual Assessment
Source Question Index: 4
Competency ID: adapter-evaluate
Bloom Level: Evaluating
Applicable: YES
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft
QUESTION FORMAT: FOUR-OPTION MCQ

QUESTION:
A class advertised as an Adapter implements the very same interface its caller already used and only records timing metrics before forwarding each call unchanged — no signatures are converted. Is the Adapter label correct?

OPTIONS:
A. Yes — any class that wraps another is an Adapter
B. Yes — forwarding calls is the essence of Adapter
C. No — because Adapters are forbidden from holding the wrapped object
D. No — with no interface conversion this adds behaviour over the same interface, which is Decorator/Proxy intent, not Adapter

CORRECT ANSWER: D. No — with no interface conversion this adds behaviour over the same interface, which is Decorator/Proxy intent, not Adapter

RATIONALE:
Adapter is defined by converting one interface into another. Keeping the same interface while adding work (metrics) is Decorator or Proxy intent; calling it Adapter misstates the intent.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Structural module content
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

================================================================Question 1
Question ID: structural-bridge:A1
Paired Question ID: structural-bridge:B1
Competency ID: br-intent
Bloom Level: Remember
Difficulty: Easy

QUESTION:
What is the intent of the Bridge pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. Add behaviour by wrapping
B. Provide a surrogate for access
C. Decouple an abstraction from its implementation so the two can vary independently
D. Compose objects into trees

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. Decouple an abstraction from its implementation so the two can vary independently

RATIONALE:
Bridge separates an abstraction from its implementation so both vary independently. The distractors are Decorator, Proxy, and Composite.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Structural module content

------------------------------------------------
Question 2
Question ID: structural-bridge:A2
Paired Question ID: structural-bridge:B2
Competency ID: br-vs-inherit
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
How does Bridge differ from plain inheritance for handling two dimensions of variation?

OPTIONS:
A. It uses one giant subclass per combination
B. It forbids polymorphism
C. It merges both dimensions into one class
D. It composes the abstraction with a separate implementor hierarchy, avoiding a combinatorial explosion of subclasses

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. It composes the abstraction with a separate implementor hierarchy, avoiding a combinatorial explosion of subclasses

RATIONALE:
Bridge composes (not inherits) abstraction with an independent implementor hierarchy, avoiding the N×M subclass explosion.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

Question 2
Question ID: structural-bridge:B2
Paired Question ID: structural-bridge:A2
Competency ID: br-vs-inherit
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
What distinguishes Bridge from the PIMPL idiom?

OPTIONS:
A. Bridge supports multiple interchangeable implementations of an abstraction, while PIMPL hides one implementation to stabilise a header
B. They are identical
C. PIMPL allows many implementations; Bridge allows only one
D. Bridge is a C++-only idiom

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Bridge supports multiple interchangeable implementations of an abstraction, while PIMPL hides one implementation to stabilise a header

RATIONALE:
Bridge’s implementor hierarchy genuinely varies; PIMPL has a single hidden implementation whose purpose is header insulation.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Structural module content

Two independently-growing dimensions (message kinds × channels) handled by composition is Bridge.

QUESTION:
The intent of Bridge is to ___.

OPTIONS:
A. add responsibilities to an object by wrapping it
B. decouple an abstraction from its implementation so the two hierarchies can vary independently
C. provide a simplified interface to a subsystem
D. share intrinsic state across many objects

CORRECT ANSWER: B. decouple an abstraction from its implementation so the two hierarchies can vary independently

RATIONALE:
Bridge separates an abstraction from its implementation so each side evolves on its own. The distractors are Decorator, Facade, and Flyweight.

* CodiNeo Structural module content

Question 2
Question ID: structural-bridge:understanding
Assessment Type: In-Module Conceptual Assessment
Source Question Index: 1
Competency ID: bridge-vs-inherit
Bloom Level: Understanding
Applicable: YES
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft
QUESTION FORMAT: FOUR-OPTION MCQ

QUESTION:
Why does Bridge use composition between an abstraction and an implementor hierarchy instead of one big inheritance tree?

OPTIONS:
A. Because composing the two hierarchies lets each grow separately and avoids an N×M explosion of combined subclasses
B. Because composition makes the abstraction a Singleton
C. Because inheritance cannot use virtual functions
D. Because it merges both dimensions into a single class

CORRECT ANSWER: A. Because composing the two hierarchies lets each grow separately and avoids an N×M explosion of combined subclasses

RATIONALE:
Bridge composes an abstraction with an independent implementor hierarchy, so adding a new abstraction or a new implementation does not require a subclass for every pairing — avoiding the combinatorial explosion inheritance would cause.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Structural module content
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

Question 3
Question ID: structural-bridge:applying
Assessment Type: In-Module Conceptual Assessment
Source Question Index: 2
Competency ID: bridge-select
Bloom Level: Applying
Applicable: YES
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft
QUESTION FORMAT: FOUR-OPTION MCQ

QUESTION:
A reporting product has report kinds (summary, audit, forecast) that must each be emitted through several output engines (PDF, HTML, spreadsheet), and both the set of report kinds and the set of engines are expected to keep growing on their own schedules. Which design avoids one class per report-kind×engine pair?

OPTIONS:
A. A surrogate that defers building each report
B. An abstraction hierarchy of report kinds that holds and delegates to a separate, independently extensible engine hierarchy
C. A class converting one engine’s interface into another’s
D. A pool that shares one report instance per kind

CORRECT ANSWER: B. An abstraction hierarchy of report kinds that holds and delegates to a separate, independently extensible engine hierarchy

RATIONALE:
Two dimensions that vary independently, joined by composition so each grows without the other, is Bridge. The distractors are Proxy, Adapter, and Flyweight.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Structural module content
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 4
Question ID: structural-bridge:analyzing
Assessment Type: In-Module Conceptual Assessment
Source Question Index: 3
Competency ID: bridge-identify
Bloom Level: Analyzing
Applicable: YES
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft
QUESTION FORMAT: FOUR-OPTION MCQ

QUESTION:
Examine:

struct Channel { virtual void deliver(const std::string&) = 0; };
struct Email : Channel { void deliver(const std::string&) override; };
struct Sms : Channel { void deliver(const std::string&) override; };
struct Alert {
  Channel* ch;
  explicit Alert(Channel* c) : ch(c) {}
  virtual void raise(const std::string& m) { ch->deliver(format(m)); }
};
struct CriticalAlert : Alert { /* refined raise */ };

Which structural pattern is this?

OPTIONS:
A. PIMPL, because Alert holds a pointer to another type
B. Decorator, because Alert wraps a Channel
C. Composite, because alerts can be nested
D. Bridge, because an abstraction hierarchy (Alert) is composed with a separate, independently varying implementor hierarchy (Channel)

CORRECT ANSWER: D. Bridge, because an abstraction hierarchy (Alert) is composed with a separate, independently varying implementor hierarchy (Channel)

RATIONALE:
Alert/CriticalAlert form an abstraction hierarchy that holds and delegates to an independent Channel implementor hierarchy; both vary independently — Bridge. It is not PIMPL (which hides one fixed implementation to stabilise a header, with no varying abstraction), not Decorator, and not Composite.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Structural module content
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------

Question ID: structural-bridge:evaluating

Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft
QUESTION FORMAT: FOUR-OPTION MCQ

QUESTION:
A class holds a single std::unique_ptr<Impl> whose only role is to keep its private members out of the public header; there is exactly one Impl and no abstraction varies. A teammate labels this “Bridge.” Is that accurate?

OPTIONS:
A. Yes — any pointer to an implementation object is Bridge
B. Yes — Bridge and PIMPL are two names for the same construct
C. No — one fixed implementation used solely for header insulation is the PIMPL idiom; Bridge requires independently varying abstraction and implementation hierarchies
D. No — because Bridge cannot legally use a smart pointer

CORRECT ANSWER: C. No — one fixed implementation used solely for header insulation is the PIMPL idiom; Bridge requires independently varying abstraction and implementation hierarchies

RATIONALE:
A single, fixed implementation pointer used only to hide members is PIMPL, a C++ idiom. Bridge requires a genuinely varying abstraction hierarchy and an independently varying implementor hierarchy — absent here.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Structural module content
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

QUESTION:
What is the intent of the Composite pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. Convert interfaces between layers
B. Add behaviour by wrapping
C. Compose objects into part-whole trees and let clients treat individual objects and compositions uniformly
D. Provide a surrogate for access control

CORRECT ANSWER:

RATIONALE:
Composite builds part-whole trees treated uniformly. The distractors are Adapter, Decorator, and Proxy.

SOURCE REFERENCES:

Question 2
Question ID: structural-composite:A2
Paired Question ID: structural-composite:B2
Competency ID: cp-uniform
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
What makes uniform treatment of leaves and composites possible?

OPTIONS:
A. Leaves inherit from composites
B. Composites store no children
C. A global registry maps node types
D. Both leaf and composite implement the same component interface

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. Both leaf and composite implement the same component interface

RATIONALE:
A shared component interface lets clients call the same operations on a leaf or a whole subtree without distinguishing them.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

A. Composite
B. Decorator
C. Proxy
D. Strategy

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Composite

RATIONALE:
Uniform treatment of single items and nested groups is Composite. The distractors add behaviour, control access, or swap algorithms.

* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

QUESTION:
Examine:  class Node { public: virtual int size() = 0; };  class File : public Node { int size() override { return sz; } };  class Folder : public Node { std::vector<Node*> kids; public: int size() override { int t = 0; for (auto* k : kids) t += k->size(); return t; } };  Which pattern is this?

OPTIONS:
A. Visitor
B. Composite
C. Decorator
D. Iterator

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. Composite

RATIONALE:
A shared Node interface where a container recursively aggregates its children is the Composite part-whole structure.

QUESTION:
A “Composite” design gives Folder a children list but File a totally different interface with unrelated method names, forcing clients to type-check each node. Does this satisfy Composite’s intent?

OPTIONS:
A. Yes — as long as there is a tree
B. Yes — composites and leaves should differ
C. No — Composite requires a shared component interface so leaf and composite are treated uniformly; type-checking defeats it
D. No — Composite forbids storing children

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. No — Composite requires a shared component interface so leaf and composite are treated uniformly; type-checking defeats it

RATIONALE:
Uniform treatment via a common interface is the whole point; if clients must branch on node type, the pattern’s benefit is lost.

* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Structural module content

Question 2
Question ID: structural-composite:understanding
Assessment Type: In-Module Conceptual Assessment
Source Question Index: 1
Competency ID: composite-uniform

Applicable: YES
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft
QUESTION FORMAT: FOUR-OPTION MCQ

QUESTION:
What makes it possible for a client to call the same operation on a single element and on a whole nested group without checking which it has?

OPTIONS:
A. The leaf type secretly inherits from the container type
B. Both the leaf and the container implement one shared component interface
C. A global registry records every node’s concrete type
D. Containers are forbidden from holding other containers

CORRECT ANSWER: B. Both the leaf and the container implement one shared component interface

RATIONALE:
A common component interface implemented by both leaf and container is what enables uniform treatment; the client never branches on node type. Recursion across sub-containers is exactly what Composite supports.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Structural module content
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

Question 3
Question ID: structural-composite:applying

QUESTION FORMAT: FOUR-OPTION MCQ

QUESTION:
A budgeting tool must answer total() for either a single line item or an envelope that contains line items and other envelopes nested to any depth, using one identical call regardless of which it is handed. Which design fits?

OPTIONS:
A. A shared component interface where containers recursively forward total() to their children
B. A wrapper that adds new behaviour to each line item
C. A surrogate that controls access to each envelope
D. A class converting envelopes into a reporting interface

CORRECT ANSWER: A. A shared component interface where containers recursively forward total() to their children

RATIONALE:
Uniform treatment of single items and arbitrarily nested groups via recursive aggregation through a shared interface is Composite. The distractors are Decorator, Proxy, and Adapter.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Structural module content
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

Question 4
Question ID: structural-composite:analyzing
Assessment Type: In-Module Conceptual Assessment
Source Question Index: 3
Competency ID: composite-identify
Bloom Level: Analyzing
Applicable: YES
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft
QUESTION FORMAT: FOUR-OPTION MCQ

QUESTION:
Examine:

struct Task { virtual int hours() = 0; };
struct Chore : Task { int hours() override { return h; } };
struct Project : Task {
  std::vector<Task*> sub;
  int hours() override {
    int t = 0; for (auto* x : sub) t += x->hours(); return t;
  }
};

Which structural pattern is this?

OPTIONS:
A. Decorator, because Project wraps Tasks
B. Bridge, because two hierarchies are involved
C. Composite, because leaf and container share one interface and the container recursively aggregates its children
D. Flyweight, because Tasks are reused

CORRECT ANSWER: C. Composite, because leaf and container share one interface and the container recursively aggregates its children

RATIONALE:
Chore (leaf) and Project (container) both implement Task, and Project recursively sums its children — the part-whole structure of Composite. There is no added responsibility (Decorator), no second varying hierarchy (Bridge), and no shared-state pooling (Flyweight).

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Structural module content
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 5
Question ID: structural-composite:evaluating
Assessment Type: In-Module Conceptual Assessment
Source Question Index: 4
Competency ID: composite-evaluate
Bloom Level: Evaluating
Applicable: YES
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft
QUESTION FORMAT: FOUR-OPTION MCQ

QUESTION:
A so-called Composite gives the container a children list and a render() method, but the leaf type exposes a wholly different method named paint() with no shared interface, so clients must test each node’s type before calling. Does this honour Composite’s intent?

OPTIONS:
A. Yes — leaves and containers are supposed to differ
B. Yes — having a tree is all Composite requires
C. No — Composite requires a shared component interface so leaf and container are used uniformly; forcing clients to type-check defeats it
D. No — Composite forbids containers from storing children

CORRECT ANSWER: C. No — Composite requires a shared component interface so leaf and container are used uniformly; forcing clients to type-check defeats it

RATIONALE:
Uniform treatment through a common interface is the whole point of Composite. If clients must branch on node type, the pattern’s benefit is lost. Containers storing children is required, not forbidden.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Structural module content
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

QUESTION:
What is the intent of the Decorator pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. Attach additional responsibilities to an object dynamically by wrapping it
B. Convert one interface into another
C. Ensure a class has one instance
D. Centralise communication between objects

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Attach additional responsibilities to an object dynamically by wrapping it

RATIONALE:
Decorator adds responsibilities to an object dynamically through wrapping. The distractors are Adapter, Singleton, and Mediator.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Structural module content

Question 2
Question ID: structural-decorator:A2
Paired Question ID: structural-decorator:B2
Competency ID: dc-stack
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Why can decorators be stacked on top of one another?

OPTIONS:
A. Because they edit the base class
B. Each decorator implements the same component interface and holds a component, so they nest
C. Because they share one global instance
D. Because they convert interfaces between layers

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. Each decorator implements the same component interface and holds a component, so they nest

RATIONALE:
Each decorator is itself a component and wraps a component, so wrappers nest indefinitely while staying interchangeable with the base.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------

Difficulty: Moderate

QUESTION:
You must add optional, combinable features (encryption, compression, buffering) to a data stream at runtime without a subclass explosion, keeping the same stream interface. Which pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. Proxy
B. Adapter
C. Decorator
D. Facade

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. Decorator

RATIONALE:
Dynamically composable responsibilities over a shared interface is Decorator. The distractors control access, convert, or simplify subsystems.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

A. Composite
B. Adapter
C. Proxy
D. Decorator

What must a decorator share with the object it wraps?

B. A single global instance

CORRECT ANSWER:

Question 2
Question ID: structural-decorator:understanding
Assessment Type: In-Module Conceptual Assessment
Source Question Index: 1
Competency ID: decorator-stack
Bloom Level: Understanding
Applicable: YES
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft
QUESTION FORMAT: FOUR-OPTION MCQ

QUESTION:
What property of a decorator allows several of them to be layered on the same object?

OPTIONS:
A. Each decorator stores a single global instance the others reuse
B. Each decorator changes the wrapped object’s interface as it nests
C. Each decorator edits the base class so later layers can attach
D. Each decorator both implements the component interface and holds a component, so the wrapper is itself decoratable

CORRECT ANSWER: D. Each decorator both implements the component interface and holds a component, so the wrapper is itself decoratable

RATIONALE:
Because a decorator is itself a component that holds a component, decorators nest indefinitely while staying interchangeable with the undecorated object. They neither edit the base class nor change the interface.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Structural module content
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

Question 3
Question ID: structural-decorator:applying
Assessment Type: In-Module Conceptual Assessment

Applicable: YES
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft
QUESTION FORMAT: FOUR-OPTION MCQ

QUESTION:
A message-sending service must let operators turn on any mix of retry, rate-limiting, and audit-logging per outgoing message at runtime, with each combination still usable wherever a plain sender is expected — and you want to avoid one subclass per combination. Which design fits?

OPTIONS:
A. A surrogate that defers creating the sender until first use
B. A class converting the sender into a third-party transport interface
C. A set of same-interface wrappers, each adding one behaviour around the forwarded send and stackable in any order
D. A simplified front that hides the sending subsystem behind one call

CORRECT ANSWER: C. A set of same-interface wrappers, each adding one behaviour around the forwarded send and stackable in any order

RATIONALE:
Composable, interchangeable add-ons over a shared interface, combined at runtime, is Decorator. The distractors are Proxy, Adapter, and Facade.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Structural module content
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 4
Question ID: structural-decorator:analyzing
Assessment Type: In-Module Conceptual Assessment
Source Question Index: 3
Competency ID: decorator-identify
Bloom Level: Analyzing
Applicable: YES
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft
QUESTION FORMAT: FOUR-OPTION MCQ

QUESTION:
Examine:

struct Report { virtual std::string body() = 0; };
struct PlainReport : Report { std::string body() override { return raw; } };
struct WatermarkLayer : Report {
  Report* inner;
  explicit WatermarkLayer(Report* r) : inner(r) {}
  std::string body() override { return inner->body() + "\n[CONFIDENTIAL]"; }
};

Which structural pattern does WatermarkLayer implement?

OPTIONS:
A. Proxy, because it wraps another Report
B. Composite, because it holds a Report
C. Adapter, because it changes the output
D. Decorator, because it shares the component interface and adds a visible new responsibility around the forwarded call, and can itself be wrapped

CORRECT ANSWER: D. Decorator, because it shares the component interface and adds a visible new responsibility around the forwarded call, and can itself be wrapped

RATIONALE:
WatermarkLayer keeps the Report interface, augments the wrapped object’s result with new client-facing content, and is itself a Report so it can stack — Decorator. Proxy would only control access; Adapter would convert the interface; Composite aggregates many children.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Structural module content
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 5
Question ID: structural-decorator:evaluating
Assessment Type: In-Module Conceptual Assessment
Source Question Index: 4
Competency ID: decorator-evaluate
Bloom Level: Evaluating
Applicable: YES
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft
QUESTION FORMAT: FOUR-OPTION MCQ

QUESTION:
A same-interface wrapper around a Connection only adds lazy opening of the socket and a permission check before delegating; it exposes no new operation or output to the caller. Is “Decorator” the most precise label?

OPTIONS:
A. No — lazy opening and access checks without any new client-facing behaviour are Proxy intent, not Decorator
B. Yes — every same-interface wrapper is a Decorator
C. Yes — lazy opening counts as a new responsibility, so Decorator
D. No — this can only be an Adapter

CORRECT ANSWER: A. No — lazy opening and access checks without any new client-facing behaviour are Proxy intent, not Decorator

RATIONALE:
Decorator specifically adds new client-facing responsibilities. Controlling access and deferring creation, with nothing new exposed, is Proxy intent. It is not interface conversion, so not Adapter.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Structural module content
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

What is the intent of the Facade pattern?

QUESTION:
Does a Facade hide or remove the subsystem’s classes?

OPTIONS:
A. It permanently removes the subsystem classes
B. It provides a simpler entry point while the subsystem classes remain accessible for advanced use

D. It guarantees only one subsystem object exists

A Facade adds a convenient interface but does not forbid direct use of the subsystem when needed.

A class exposes one boot() method that calls several subsystem objects in sequence so callers don’t deal with them directly, but the subsystems don’t communicate back through it. Best classified as?

QUESTION:
A so-called Facade not only simplifies calls but also forces all subsystem objects to communicate exclusively through it, coordinating their mutual interactions. Is “Facade” still the most precise label?

OPTIONS:
A. Yes — Facade always coordinates communication
B. No — centralising mutual communication between objects is Mediator’s intent, not Facade’s
C. Yes — Facade and Mediator are identical
D. No — this is actually Adapter

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. No — centralising mutual communication between objects is Mediator’s intent, not Facade’s

RATIONALE:

================================================================Question 1
Question ID: structural-facade:remembering
Assessment Type: In-Module Conceptual Assessment
Source Question Index: 0
Competency ID: facade-intent
Bloom Level: Remembering
Applicable: YES
Difficulty: Easy
Validation Status: draft
QUESTION FORMAT: FOUR-OPTION MCQ

QUESTION:
A Facade is introduced in order to ___.

OPTIONS:
A. offer a single simplified interface over a larger or more complex subsystem
B. convert one existing interface into another
C. attach extra responsibilities to an object dynamically
D. guarantee a class has exactly one instance

CORRECT ANSWER: A. offer a single simplified interface over a larger or more complex subsystem

RATIONALE:
Facade provides a simplified front-door interface to a subsystem. The distractors are Adapter, Decorator, and Singleton.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Structural module content
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 2
Question ID: structural-facade:understanding
Assessment Type: In-Module Conceptual Assessment
Source Question Index: 1
Competency ID: facade-access
Bloom Level: Understanding
Applicable: YES
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft
QUESTION FORMAT: FOUR-OPTION MCQ

QUESTION:
After a Facade is added, what happens to the underlying subsystem classes?

OPTIONS:
A. They remain reachable for advanced needs; the Facade only adds a convenient high-level entry point
B. They are deleted, since the Facade replaces them
C. Each of their interfaces is converted into the Facade’s interface
D. They are reduced to a single shared instance

CORRECT ANSWER: A. They remain reachable for advanced needs; the Facade only adds a convenient high-level entry point

RATIONALE:
A Facade is additive: it simplifies common usage while leaving the subsystem classes accessible when finer control is required. It neither removes them nor converts their interfaces.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Structural module content
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 3
Question ID: structural-facade:applying
Assessment Type: In-Module Conceptual Assessment
Source Question Index: 2
Competency ID: facade-select
Bloom Level: Applying
Applicable: YES
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft
QUESTION FORMAT: FOUR-OPTION MCQ

QUESTION:
A checkout button should trigger one call that internally reserves stock, charges the card, books a courier, and emails a receipt, so callers never touch those four components directly and the components do not call back through it. Which design fits?

OPTIONS:
A. A surrogate that controls access to each of the four components
B. A wrapper that adds new behaviour to each component in a stack
C. A hub through which the four components route all their mutual messages
D. One class that owns the four components and exposes a single high-level method choreographing them one-way

CORRECT ANSWER: D. One class that owns the four components and exposes a single high-level method choreographing them one-way

RATIONALE:
A single class exposing one high-level method that orchestrates several subsystem objects one-way is a Facade. The distractors are Proxy, Decorator, and Mediator (which would coordinate two-way peer communication).

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Structural module content
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 4
Question ID: structural-facade:analyzing
Assessment Type: In-Module Conceptual Assessment
Source Question Index: 3
Competency ID: facade-identify
Bloom Level: Analyzing
Applicable: YES
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft
QUESTION FORMAT: FOUR-OPTION MCQ

QUESTION:
Examine:

struct MediaExport {
  Decoder dec; Resampler res; Encoder enc; Muxer mux;
  void run(const Clip& c) {
    auto frames = dec.decode(c);
    auto fixed  = res.normalize(frames);
    auto stream = enc.encode(fixed);
    mux.write(stream);
  }
};

Which structural pattern does MediaExport illustrate?

OPTIONS:
A. Mediator, because four components are involved
B. Adapter, because it converts the clip
C. Facade, because one class exposes a single high-level method that choreographs several subsystem objects one-way
D. Composite, because the steps form a tree

CORRECT ANSWER: C. Facade, because one class exposes a single high-level method that choreographs several subsystem objects one-way

RATIONALE:
MediaExport wraps a multi-step subsystem interaction behind one simple method (run), calling the components in sequence without them communicating back through it — a Facade. Mediator would govern two-way peer communication; Adapter converts interfaces; Composite builds part-whole trees.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Structural module content
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 5
Question ID: structural-facade:evaluating
Assessment Type: In-Module Conceptual Assessment
Source Question Index: 4
Competency ID: facade-evaluate
Bloom Level: Evaluating
Applicable: YES
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft
QUESTION FORMAT: FOUR-OPTION MCQ

QUESTION:
A class billed as a Facade not only offers simple entry methods but also forces every subsystem object to route all of its mutual messages through it, coordinating their back-and-forth interactions. Is “Facade” still the most precise label?

OPTIONS:
A. Yes — Facade is responsible for coordinating communication
B. No — centralising mutual, two-way communication among peers is Mediator’s intent; Facade is strictly one-way simplification
C. Yes — Facade and Mediator describe the same role
D. No — this is actually an Adapter

CORRECT ANSWER: B. No — centralising mutual, two-way communication among peers is Mediator’s intent; Facade is strictly one-way simplification

RATIONALE:
Once a class governs two-way communication among a set of peers, its intent matches Mediator. A Facade only simplifies one-way access to a subsystem; it does not mediate peer interactions.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Structural module content
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
