================================================================Question 1
Question ID: structural-flyweight:A1
Paired Question ID: structural-flyweight:B1
Competency ID: fw-intent
Bloom Level: Remember
Difficulty: Easy
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
What is the intent of the Flyweight pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. Convert interfaces
B. Add behaviour by wrapping
C. Share common state to support large numbers of fine-grained objects efficiently
D. Provide a surrogate for access

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. Share common state to support large numbers of fine-grained objects efficiently

RATIONALE:
Flyweight minimises memory by sharing state across many similar objects. The distractors are Adapter, Decorator, and Proxy.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Structural module content

------------------------------------------------
Question 2
Question ID: structural-flyweight:A2
Paired Question ID: structural-flyweight:B2
Competency ID: fw-state
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
What is the difference between intrinsic and extrinsic state in Flyweight?

OPTIONS:
A. Intrinsic is per-client; extrinsic is shared
B. Both are stored in the flyweight
C. Both are supplied by the client each call
D. Intrinsic state is shared and stored in the flyweight; extrinsic state is context-dependent and supplied by the client

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. Intrinsic state is shared and stored in the flyweight; extrinsic state is context-dependent and supplied by the client

RATIONALE:
Intrinsic (shared) state lives in the flyweight; extrinsic (context) state is passed in by the client per operation.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 3
Question ID: structural-flyweight:A3
Paired Question ID: structural-flyweight:B3
Competency ID: fw-select
Bloom Level: Apply
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A text editor renders millions of character glyphs; each glyph’s font and shape repeat, but its position differs. How do you cut memory by sharing the repeating data?

OPTIONS:
A. Flyweight
B. Composite
C. Proxy
D. Decorator

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Flyweight

RATIONALE:
Sharing repeated intrinsic data while supplying per-use position is Flyweight. The distractors build trees, control access, or add behaviour.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 4
Question ID: structural-flyweight:A4
Paired Question ID: structural-flyweight:B4
Competency ID: fw-identify
Bloom Level: Analyze
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Examine:  class Glyph { char c; Font* font; public: void draw(int x, int y); };  Glyph* GlyphFactory::get(char c) { if (!pool.count(c)) pool[c] = new Glyph(c); return pool[c]; }  Position (x, y) is passed to draw(). Which pattern is this?

OPTIONS:
A. Prototype
B. Flyweight
C. Singleton
D. Adapter

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. Flyweight

RATIONALE:
A factory pooling shared instances by intrinsic key (char/font) while position is passed in (extrinsic) is Flyweight.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 5
Question ID: structural-flyweight:A5
Paired Question ID: structural-flyweight:B5
Competency ID: fw-evaluate
Bloom Level: Evaluate
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A “Flyweight” stores each object’s unique screen position as a member and never shares instances, creating one object per character. Does this achieve Flyweight’s goal?

OPTIONS:
A. Yes — Flyweight stores all state internally
B. Yes — one object per character is the goal
C. No — keeping per-instance extrinsic state and not sharing defeats Flyweight; position should be extrinsic and instances shared
D. No — Flyweight forbids a factory

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. No — keeping per-instance extrinsic state and not sharing defeats Flyweight; position should be extrinsic and instances shared

RATIONALE:
Flyweight’s memory savings come from sharing instances and externalising context state; storing position per instance and not sharing negates the benefit.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Structural module content

QUESTION:
Flyweight is used to ___.

OPTIONS:
A. encapsulate a request as an object
B. define interchangeable algorithms
C. traverse a collection
D. support large numbers of similar objects efficiently by sharing their common state

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. support large numbers of similar objects efficiently by sharing their common state

RATIONALE:
Flyweight supports many similar objects by sharing common state. The distractors are Command, Strategy, and Iterator.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Structural module content

------------------------------------------------
Question 2
Question ID: structural-flyweight:B2
Paired Question ID: structural-flyweight:A2
Competency ID: fw-state
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Where is extrinsic state kept in a Flyweight design?

OPTIONS:
A. Outside the flyweight — supplied by the client or context when an operation runs
B. Permanently inside each shared flyweight
C. In a single global Singleton only
D. Nowhere — extrinsic state is discarded

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Outside the flyweight — supplied by the client or context when an operation runs

RATIONALE:
Extrinsic state is context-dependent and provided by the client at call time, so it must not be stored in the shared flyweight.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------

QUESTION:
A forest scene draws 1,000,000 trees of a few species; mesh and texture repeat per species while location varies per tree. How do you avoid storing the mesh a million times?

OPTIONS:
A. Composite
B. Flyweight
C. Bridge
D. Memento

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. Flyweight

RATIONALE:
Sharing per-species intrinsic data while passing per-tree position is Flyweight.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

QUESTION:
Examine:  class TreeType { std::string name; Texture tex; public: void render(int x, int y); };  TreeType* TreeFactory::of(std::string n) { auto it = pool.find(n); if (it != pool.end()) return it->second; return pool[n] = new TreeType(n); }  Which pattern is this?

OPTIONS:
A. Prototype
B. Builder
C. Flyweight
D. Proxy

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. Flyweight

RATIONALE:
A factory returning shared instances keyed by intrinsic data, with position passed to render(), is Flyweight.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

Question 5
Question ID: structural-flyweight:B5
Paired Question ID: structural-flyweight:A5
Competency ID: fw-evaluate
Bloom Level: Evaluate
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A renderer keeps one shared ParticleType (color, sprite) per kind and passes each particle’s x/y/velocity at draw time. Is this a correct Flyweight?

OPTIONS:
A. No — Flyweight must store position internally
B. No — Flyweight cannot use a factory
C. Yes — but only for fewer than 100 objects
D. Yes — sharing intrinsic data while passing extrinsic state at call time is exactly Flyweight

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. Yes — sharing intrinsic data while passing extrinsic state at call time is exactly Flyweight

RATIONALE:
Shared intrinsic data plus client-supplied extrinsic state at call time is the correct Flyweight design.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Structural module content

QUESTION FORMAT: FOUR-OPTION MCQ

QUESTION:
In this module, the main goal of the Flyweight pattern is to ___.

OPTIONS:
A. compose objects into part-whole trees
B. decouple an abstraction from its implementation
C. provide a controlled surrogate for a costly object
D. support very large numbers of similar objects efficiently by sharing their common state

CORRECT ANSWER: D. support very large numbers of similar objects efficiently by sharing their common state

RATIONALE:
Flyweight reduces memory by sharing common (intrinsic) state across many fine-grained objects. The distractors are Composite, Bridge, and Proxy.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Structural module content
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

Question 2
Question ID: structural-flyweight:understanding
Assessment Type: In-Module Conceptual Assessment
Source Question Index: 1
Competency ID: flyweight-state
Bloom Level: Understanding
Applicable: YES
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft
QUESTION FORMAT: FOUR-OPTION MCQ

QUESTION:
In a Flyweight design, where do intrinsic and extrinsic state respectively live?

OPTIONS:
A. Intrinsic state is shared and stored inside the flyweight; extrinsic state is context-dependent and supplied by the client at call time
B. Both intrinsic and extrinsic state are stored inside each shared flyweight
C. Both are passed in by the client on every call and never stored
D. Intrinsic state is per-client; extrinsic state is the shared part

CORRECT ANSWER: A. Intrinsic state is shared and stored inside the flyweight; extrinsic state is context-dependent and supplied by the client at call time

RATIONALE:
Intrinsic (shared) state lives in the flyweight; extrinsic (context) state must be supplied by the client per operation so it is not duplicated across the shared instances.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Structural module content
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 3
Question ID: structural-flyweight:applying
Assessment Type: In-Module Conceptual Assessment
Source Question Index: 2
Competency ID: flyweight-select
Bloom Level: Applying
Applicable: YES
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft
QUESTION FORMAT: FOUR-OPTION MCQ

QUESTION:
A tile-based game draws millions of map cells drawn from a handful of terrain kinds; each kind’s sprite and movement-cost repeat across cells, while each cell’s grid coordinates differ. How do you avoid storing the sprite data once per cell?

OPTIONS:
A. Build a tree of cells treated uniformly
B. Keep one shared object per terrain kind holding the repeating data, and pass each cell’s coordinates in at draw time
C. Wrap each cell in a behaviour-adding layer
D. Give each cell a surrogate that controls access

CORRECT ANSWER: B. Keep one shared object per terrain kind holding the repeating data, and pass each cell’s coordinates in at draw time

RATIONALE:
Sharing the repeating intrinsic data per terrain kind while passing per-cell position (extrinsic) at call time is Flyweight. The distractors are Composite, Decorator, and Proxy.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Structural module content
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 4
Question ID: structural-flyweight:analyzing
Assessment Type: In-Module Conceptual Assessment
Source Question Index: 3
Competency ID: flyweight-identify
Bloom Level: Analyzing
Applicable: YES
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft
QUESTION FORMAT: FOUR-OPTION MCQ

QUESTION:
Examine:

struct Particle { Color color; Sprite sprite; void draw(float x, float y, float vx, float vy); };
struct ParticlePool {
  std::unordered_map<std::string, Particle*> kinds;
  Particle* get(const std::string& k) {
    auto it = kinds.find(k);
    if (it != kinds.end()) return it->second;
    return kinds[k] = new Particle(k);
  }
};

Given that position and velocity are passed to draw(), which structural pattern is this?

OPTIONS:
A. Prototype, because new particles are produced
B. Flyweight, because a pool hands out shared instances keyed by intrinsic state while per-call context (position, velocity) is passed in
C. Singleton, because the pool is shared
D. Proxy, because get() stands in for a particle

CORRECT ANSWER: B. Flyweight, because a pool hands out shared instances keyed by intrinsic state while per-call context (position, velocity) is passed in

RATIONALE:
ParticlePool returns shared instances keyed by intrinsic data (color/sprite) and the per-use position/velocity is passed to draw() as extrinsic state — Flyweight. It is not cloning (Prototype), a single global instance (Singleton), or access control (Proxy).

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Structural module content
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 5
Question ID: structural-flyweight:evaluating
Assessment Type: In-Module Conceptual Assessment
Source Question Index: 4
Competency ID: flyweight-evaluate
Bloom Level: Evaluating
Applicable: YES
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft
QUESTION FORMAT: FOUR-OPTION MCQ

QUESTION:
A design described as Flyweight stores each glyph’s on-screen x/y as a member field and creates a brand-new object for every character on the page, sharing nothing. Does this achieve Flyweight’s goal?

OPTIONS:
A. Yes — a Flyweight is supposed to store all state internally
B. Yes — one object per character is the intended outcome
C. No — keeping per-instance extrinsic state (position) and never sharing instances negates Flyweight; position should be extrinsic and instances shared
D. No — because Flyweight is not allowed to use a factory or pool

CORRECT ANSWER: C. No — keeping per-instance extrinsic state (position) and never sharing instances negates Flyweight; position should be extrinsic and instances shared

RATIONALE:
Flyweight’s savings come from sharing instances and externalising context state. Storing each glyph’s position internally and creating one object per character defeats the purpose. A factory/pool is in fact a normal part of Flyweight.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Structural module content
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

================================================================Question 1
Question ID: structural-proxy:A1
Paired Question ID: structural-proxy:B1
Competency ID: px-intent
Bloom Level: Remember
Difficulty: Easy
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
What is the intent of the Proxy pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. Convert one interface into another
B. Add behaviour by stacking wrappers
C. Provide a surrogate or placeholder that controls access to another object
D. Compose objects into part-whole trees

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. Provide a surrogate or placeholder that controls access to another object

RATIONALE:
Proxy supplies a stand-in that controls access to a real subject. The distractors are Adapter, Decorator, and Composite.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Structural module content

------------------------------------------------
Question 2
Question ID: structural-proxy:A2
Paired Question ID: structural-proxy:B2
Competency ID: px-relate
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
How does a proxy typically relate to its real subject?

OPTIONS:
A. It exposes a different interface than the subject
B. It inherits the subject’s private data directly
C. It replaces the subject permanently
D. It implements the same interface as the subject and forwards requests, optionally adding access control

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. It implements the same interface as the subject and forwards requests, optionally adding access control

RATIONALE:
A proxy shares the subject’s interface so it is interchangeable, forwarding calls while controlling access (lazy load, protection, remoting).

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 3
Question ID: structural-proxy:A3
Paired Question ID: structural-proxy:B3
Competency ID: px-select
Bloom Level: Apply
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A large image is expensive to load; you want a stand-in that exposes the same interface but defers loading until the image is actually drawn. Which pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. Proxy
B. Decorator
C. Adapter
D. Composite

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Proxy

RATIONALE:
A same-interface placeholder that delays creation of the real object is a (virtual) Proxy. The distractors add behaviour, convert, or build trees.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 4
Question ID: structural-proxy:A4
Paired Question ID: structural-proxy:B4
Competency ID: px-identify
Bloom Level: Analyze
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Examine:  class Image { public: virtual void draw() = 0; };  class ProxyImage : public Image { RealImage* real = nullptr; std::string file; public: void draw() override { if (!real) real = new RealImage(file); real->draw(); } };  Which pattern is this?

OPTIONS:
A. Adapter
B. Proxy
C. Flyweight
D. Bridge

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. Proxy

RATIONALE:
Same-interface wrapper that lazily creates and delegates to the real subject is a virtual Proxy.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 5
Question ID: structural-proxy:A5
Paired Question ID: structural-proxy:B5
Competency ID: px-evaluate
Bloom Level: Evaluate
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A wrapper implements the subject’s interface and adds logging plus access checks before delegating, without changing the operation’s result or adding new client-facing features. Best classified as?

OPTIONS:
A. Decorator — any wrapper that does extra work is a Decorator
B. Adapter — it converts the interface
C. Proxy — it controls access to the same operation without adding new client-facing behaviour
D. Composite — it manages children

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. Proxy — it controls access to the same operation without adding new client-facing behaviour

RATIONALE:
Controlling access (auth/logging) to the same operation, without new client-facing responsibilities, is Proxy intent, distinguishing it from Decorator.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Structural module content

================================================================Question 1
Question ID: structural-proxy:B1
Paired Question ID: structural-proxy:A1
Competency ID: px-intent
Bloom Level: Remember
Difficulty: Easy
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Proxy’s core purpose is to ___.

OPTIONS:
A. share state among many fine-grained objects
B. define a family of interchangeable algorithms
C. simplify a subsystem behind one interface
D. act as a controlled stand-in for another object

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. act as a controlled stand-in for another object

RATIONALE:
Proxy is a controlled surrogate for another object. The distractors are Flyweight, Strategy, and Facade.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Structural module content

------------------------------------------------
Question 2
Question ID: structural-proxy:B2
Paired Question ID: structural-proxy:A2
Competency ID: px-relate
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
What distinguishes a Proxy from a Decorator in intent?

OPTIONS:
A. A Proxy controls or manages access to the subject, while a Decorator adds new responsibilities
B. They are identical in intent
C. A Proxy always adds new features
D. A Decorator changes the object’s interface

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. A Proxy controls or manages access to the subject, while a Decorator adds new responsibilities

RATIONALE:
Both wrap a same-interface object, but Proxy’s intent is access control/management whereas Decorator’s is adding behaviour.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 3
Question ID: structural-proxy:B3
Paired Question ID: structural-proxy:A3
Competency ID: px-select
Bloom Level: Apply
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Remote service calls are costly; you want a local object with the same interface that transparently handles the connection and caches results. Which pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. Flyweight
B. Proxy
C. Adapter
D. Mediator

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. Proxy

RATIONALE:
A same-interface local stand-in for a remote object (managing connection/caching) is a remote Proxy.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 4
Question ID: structural-proxy:B4
Paired Question ID: structural-proxy:A4
Competency ID: px-identify
Bloom Level: Analyze
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Examine:  class BankAccount { public: virtual void withdraw(int) = 0; };  class ProtectedAccount : public BankAccount { RealAccount acc; User u; public: void withdraw(int n) override { if (u.isOwner()) acc.withdraw(n); } };  Which pattern is this?

OPTIONS:
A. Decorator
B. Bridge
C. Proxy
D. Strategy

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. Proxy

RATIONALE:
A same-interface wrapper that performs an access check before delegating is a protection Proxy.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 5
Question ID: structural-proxy:B5
Paired Question ID: structural-proxy:A5
Competency ID: px-evaluate
Bloom Level: Evaluate
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A class wraps a Database with the same interface and simply forwards every call unchanged — no access control, lazy initialization, or remoting. Is “Proxy” the right label?

OPTIONS:
A. Yes — any same-interface wrapper is a Proxy
B. Yes — forwarding is the whole point
C. No — Proxy requires changing the interface
D. No — with no access control, lazy loading, or remoting it serves no proxy purpose

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. No — with no access control, lazy loading, or remoting it serves no proxy purpose

RATIONALE:
A pure pass-through adds none of the access-management responsibilities that define a Proxy, so the label is not meaningful here.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Structural module content

Question ID: structural-proxy:remembering

Source Question Index: 0
Competency ID: proxy-intent
Bloom Level: Remembering
Applicable: YES
Difficulty: Easy

QUESTION FORMAT: FOUR-OPTION MCQ

QUESTION:
The defining purpose of a Proxy is to ___.

OPTIONS:
A. compose objects into part-whole trees
B. decouple an abstraction from its implementation
C. provide a same-interface surrogate that controls access to a real object
D. convert one interface into another the caller expects

CORRECT ANSWER: C. provide a same-interface surrogate that controls access to a real object

RATIONALE:
Proxy supplies a stand-in that controls access to the real subject. The distractors are Composite, Bridge, and Adapter.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Structural module content
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 2
Question ID: structural-proxy:understanding
Assessment Type: In-Module Conceptual Assessment
Source Question Index: 1
Competency ID: proxy-relate
Bloom Level: Understanding
Applicable: YES
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft
QUESTION FORMAT: FOUR-OPTION MCQ

QUESTION:
Why must a proxy expose the same interface as the real subject it guards?

OPTIONS:
A. So it can be substituted for the subject transparently while it decides whether and when to forward calls
B. So it can offer the caller brand-new operations the subject lacks
C. So it can translate the subject into an unrelated interface
D. So that it can store all of the subject’s state internally

CORRECT ANSWER: A. So it can be substituted for the subject transparently while it decides whether and when to forward calls

RATIONALE:
Sharing the subject’s interface lets the proxy be used wherever the subject is, transparently controlling access (lazy load, auth, remoting). Adding new operations would be Decorator; translating would be Adapter.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Structural module content
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 3
Question ID: structural-proxy:applying
Assessment Type: In-Module Conceptual Assessment
Source Question Index: 2
Competency ID: proxy-select
Bloom Level: Applying
Applicable: YES
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft
QUESTION FORMAT: FOUR-OPTION MCQ

QUESTION:
A reporting screen references thousands of high-resolution attachments, but most are never opened. You want each attachment reference to behave exactly like the real document object yet defer the costly disk read until something actually calls render(). What design fits?

OPTIONS:
A. A wrapper that adds new export buttons to the document
B. A same-interface placeholder that loads the real document only on first use
C. A class converting the document into a printer interface
D. A shared pool that stores one document per file type

CORRECT ANSWER: B. A same-interface placeholder that loads the real document only on first use

RATIONALE:
A same-interface stand-in that postpones creating/loading the real object until it is used is a virtual Proxy. The distractors are Decorator, Adapter, and Flyweight.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Structural module content
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 4
Question ID: structural-proxy:analyzing
Assessment Type: In-Module Conceptual Assessment
Source Question Index: 3
Competency ID: proxy-identify
Bloom Level: Analyzing
Applicable: YES
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft
QUESTION FORMAT: FOUR-OPTION MCQ

QUESTION:
Examine:

struct Vault { virtual std::string read(int slot) = 0; };
struct GuardedVault : Vault {
  RealVault real; Session s;
  std::string read(int slot) override {
    if (!s.hasClearance(slot)) throw AccessDenied{};
    return real.read(slot);
  }
};

Which structural pattern is GuardedVault?

OPTIONS:
A. Decorator, because it does extra work before forwarding
B. Adapter, because it wraps another object
C. Composite, because it manages stored slots
D. Proxy, because it shares the subject’s interface and gates access without adding new client-facing operations

CORRECT ANSWER: D. Proxy, because it shares the subject’s interface and gates access without adding new client-facing operations

RATIONALE:
GuardedVault keeps the same Vault interface and performs an access check before delegating, adding no new client-facing feature — a protection Proxy. Decorator would add a visible new responsibility; Adapter would convert the interface.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Structural module content
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 5
Question ID: structural-proxy:evaluating
Assessment Type: In-Module Conceptual Assessment
Source Question Index: 4
Competency ID: proxy-evaluate
Bloom Level: Evaluating
Applicable: YES
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft
QUESTION FORMAT: FOUR-OPTION MCQ

QUESTION:
A class wraps a RemoteLedger with an identical interface but does nothing except call through to it on every method — no lazy loading, no caching, no access checks, no remoting setup. Is “Proxy” a meaningful classification here?

OPTIONS:
A. Yes — any same-interface pass-through is a Proxy
B. Yes — forwarding alone fulfils Proxy’s intent
C. No — a Proxy must convert the subject’s interface, which this does not
D. No — without any access management (lazy load, caching, protection, remoting) it serves no proxy purpose

CORRECT ANSWER: D. No — without any access management (lazy load, caching, protection, remoting) it serves no proxy purpose

RATIONALE:
Proxy is defined by some form of access control or management. A pure pass-through provides none, so the label is hollow. Proxy does not convert interfaces — that is Adapter.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Structural module content
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

================================================================Question 1
Question ID: structural-repository:A1
Paired Question ID: structural-repository:B1
Competency ID: rp-intent
Bloom Level: Remember
Difficulty: Easy
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
What is the purpose of the Repository pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. Provide a collection-like interface that mediates between domain logic and data storage, hiding persistence details
B. Convert one interface into another
C. Ensure a class has one instance
D. Share intrinsic state across objects

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Provide a collection-like interface that mediates between domain logic and data storage, hiding persistence details

RATIONALE:
Repository gives domain code a clean, collection-like interface over storage, hiding persistence. The distractors are Adapter, Singleton, and Flyweight.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Structural module content
* Fowler (2002), Patterns of Enterprise Application Architecture — Repository
* Evans (2003), Domain-Driven Design

------------------------------------------------
Question 2
Question ID: structural-repository:A2
Paired Question ID: structural-repository:B2
Competency ID: rp-classify
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Repository is NOT one of the original Gang-of-Four design patterns. How is it best classified?

OPTIONS:
A. As a GoF structural pattern like Adapter
B. As an enterprise/application architecture pattern for data access, outside the GoF catalog
C. As a GoF creational pattern
D. As a C++ language idiom

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. As an enterprise/application architecture pattern for data access, outside the GoF catalog

RATIONALE:
Repository comes from enterprise/application architecture (Fowler, Evans), not the GoF catalog; it is an application-architecture data-access pattern.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Structural module content
* Fowler (2002), Patterns of Enterprise Application Architecture — Repository
* Evans (2003), Domain-Driven Design

------------------------------------------------

QUESTION:
Domain code should query and save User objects without knowing whether they live in SQL, a file, or an in-memory store, so the data source can be swapped freely. Which approach fits?

OPTIONS:
A. Singleton access
B. Decorator wrapping
C. A Repository abstraction over storage
D. A Facade over the UI

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. A Repository abstraction over storage

RATIONALE:
A domain-facing interface that hides and allows swapping the storage mechanism is the Repository approach.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Structural module content
* Fowler (2002), Patterns of Enterprise Application Architecture — Repository
* Evans (2003), Domain-Driven Design

A. Interface conversion (Adapter)
B. Access control (Proxy)
C. Algorithm selection (Strategy)
D. A Repository abstracting persistence behind a domain-facing interface

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. A Repository abstracting persistence behind a domain-facing interface

A domain-facing data-access interface with interchangeable backing stores is the Repository pattern (not GoF).

QUESTION:
A class named UserRepository builds SQL strings throughout the domain layer and exposes raw connection objects to callers. Does this honor the Repository idea?

OPTIONS:
A. No — Repository’s purpose is to hide persistence behind a clean interface; leaking SQL and connections to the domain defeats the separation
B. Yes — the name makes it a Repository
C. Yes — exposing connections is required
D. No — Repository must be a GoF pattern

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. No — Repository’s purpose is to hide persistence behind a clean interface; leaking SQL and connections to the domain defeats the separation

RATIONALE:
Repository exists to separate domain logic from data-access concerns; leaking SQL/connections breaks that separation regardless of the class name.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Structural module content
* Fowler (2002), Patterns of Enterprise Application Architecture — Repository
* Evans (2003), Domain-Driven Design

QUESTION:
Which statement about Repository’s classification is accurate?

OPTIONS:
A. It is the GoF Bridge pattern under another name
B. It is a GoF structural pattern with automated detector support
C. It is a supplemental enterprise architecture pattern, not part of the GoF set, and has no automated GoF-style detector here
D. It is identical to the Proxy pattern

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. It is a supplemental enterprise architecture pattern, not part of the GoF set, and has no automated GoF-style detector here

Repository is an enterprise architecture pattern (not GoF), and CodiNeo provides no automated GoF-style detector for it — conceptual assessment only.

QUESTION:
Tests must run against an in-memory data store while production uses a real database, with identical domain code. Which design enables this substitution cleanly?

OPTIONS:
A. A Singleton database
B. A Decorator chain
C. A Facade
D. A Repository interface with interchangeable implementations

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. A Repository interface with interchangeable implementations

RATIONALE:
A Repository interface lets domain code stay the same while the backing store is swapped (in-memory for tests, DB for production).

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Structural module content
* Fowler (2002), Patterns of Enterprise Application Architecture — Repository
* Evans (2003), Domain-Driven Design

QUESTION:
A team adds a Repository interface, but every method returns raw database rows and requires callers to manage transactions and SQL. Is this a faithful Repository?

OPTIONS:
A. Yes — Repositories should expose SQL
B. No — leaking raw rows, transactions, and SQL to callers breaks the abstraction the pattern exists to provide
C. Yes — as long as it is named Repository
D. No — Repositories may not return data at all

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. No — leaking raw rows, transactions, and SQL to callers breaks the abstraction the pattern exists to provide

RATIONALE:
Repository must insulate the domain from data-access mechanics; exposing rows/transactions/SQL defeats its purpose.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Structural module content
* Fowler (2002), Patterns of Enterprise Application Architecture — Repository
* Evans (2003), Domain-Driven Design

Question 2
Question ID: structural-repository:understanding

Source Question Index: 1
Competency ID: repository-classify

Applicable: YES
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft
QUESTION FORMAT: FOUR-OPTION MCQ

QUESTION:
How should Repository be classified relative to the original Gang-of-Four catalog?

OPTIONS:
A. As a GoF structural pattern, alongside Adapter and Proxy
B. As a GoF creational pattern for building entities
C. As a C++ language idiom like PIMPL
D. As a supplemental enterprise / application-architecture pattern for data access, outside the GoF set

CORRECT ANSWER: D. As a supplemental enterprise / application-architecture pattern for data access, outside the GoF set

RATIONALE:
Repository originates in enterprise/application architecture (Fowler; Evans) and is not part of the GoF catalog; it is an application-architecture data-access pattern, not a GoF structural pattern or a language idiom.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Structural module content
* Fowler (2002), Patterns of Enterprise Application Architecture — Repository
* Evans (2003), Domain-Driven Design

Question 3
Question ID: structural-repository:applying
Assessment Type: In-Module Conceptual Assessment
Source Question Index: 2
Competency ID: repository-select
Bloom Level: Applying
Applicable: YES
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft
QUESTION FORMAT: FOUR-OPTION MCQ

QUESTION:
Your service code must create, fetch, and delete Invoice entities identically whether they live in PostgreSQL in production or an in-memory map during tests, with the service code never mentioning SQL or connections. Which approach achieves this cleanly?

OPTIONS:
A. Make the database a single global instance the service reads directly
B. Define an Invoice-storage interface (findById, save, delete) with interchangeable backing implementations behind it
C. Wrap each Invoice in a behaviour-adding layer
D. Put a simplified front over the application’s user interface

CORRECT ANSWER: B. Define an Invoice-storage interface (findById, save, delete) with interchangeable backing implementations behind it

RATIONALE:
A domain-facing storage interface with swappable implementations (real DB vs in-memory) that keeps persistence details out of the domain is the Repository approach. The distractors are Singleton, Decorator, and Facade-over-UI.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Structural module content
* Fowler (2002), Patterns of Enterprise Application Architecture — Repository
* Evans (2003), Domain-Driven Design

------------------------------------------------
Question 4
Question ID: structural-repository:analyzing
Assessment Type: In-Module Conceptual Assessment
Source Question Index: 3
Competency ID: repository-identify
Bloom Level: Analyzing
Applicable: YES
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft
QUESTION FORMAT: FOUR-OPTION MCQ

QUESTION:
Examine:

struct Customer { /* domain entity */ };
struct CustomerStore {
  virtual std::optional<Customer> findById(int) = 0;
  virtual void save(const Customer&) = 0;
};
struct SqlCustomerStore : CustomerStore { /* SQL */ };
struct MemoryCustomerStore : CustomerStore { /* std::map */ };

What does this structure most directly demonstrate?

OPTIONS:
A. A Repository: a domain-facing, collection-like interface that hides and lets you swap the persistence mechanism
B. An Adapter converting one storage interface into another
C. A Proxy controlling access to the database
D. A Bridge varying two independent hierarchies

CORRECT ANSWER: A. A Repository: a domain-facing, collection-like interface that hides and lets you swap the persistence mechanism

RATIONALE:
A domain-facing interface (findById/save) with interchangeable backing stores (SQL, in-memory) that conceals persistence is the Repository pattern — a non-GoF enterprise pattern. It is not interface conversion (Adapter), access control (Proxy), or two co-varying hierarchies (Bridge).

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Structural module content
* Fowler (2002), Patterns of Enterprise Application Architecture — Repository
* Evans (2003), Domain-Driven Design

------------------------------------------------
Question 5
Question ID: structural-repository:evaluating
Assessment Type: In-Module Conceptual Assessment
Source Question Index: 4
Competency ID: repository-evaluate
Bloom Level: Evaluating
Applicable: YES
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft
QUESTION FORMAT: FOUR-OPTION MCQ

QUESTION:
A class named OrderRepository exposes methods that return raw result-set cursors and require each caller to open transactions and assemble SQL fragments. Does this faithfully realise the Repository idea?

OPTIONS:
A. Yes — the name OrderRepository makes it a Repository
B. Yes — a Repository is supposed to surface SQL and connections
C. No — leaking cursors, transactions, and SQL to callers breaks the domain/persistence separation the pattern exists to provide
D. No — a Repository may not return any data to callers

CORRECT ANSWER: C. No — leaking cursors, transactions, and SQL to callers breaks the domain/persistence separation the pattern exists to provide

RATIONALE:
Repository exists to insulate domain code from data-access mechanics. Exposing cursors, transactions, and SQL defeats that separation regardless of the class name. Returning entities is exactly what it should do — just not raw rows.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Structural module content
* Fowler (2002), Patterns of Enterprise Application Architecture — Repository
* Evans (2003), Domain-Driven Design

QUESTION:
What is the intent of the Chain of Responsibility pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. Pass a request along a chain of handlers until one handles it, decoupling sender from receiver
B. Convert interfaces
C. Ensure one instance
D. Share intrinsic state

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Pass a request along a chain of handlers until one handles it, decoupling sender from receiver

RATIONALE:
Chain of Responsibility forwards a request through handlers until one handles it. The distractors are Adapter, Singleton, and Flyweight.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Behavioural module content

QUESTION:
Examine:  class Handler { protected: Handler* next = nullptr; public: void setNext(Handler* n) { next = n; } virtual void handle(int lvl) { if (next) next->handle(lvl); } };  class Warn : public Handler { void handle(int lvl) override { if (lvl < 2) log("warn"); else Handler::handle(lvl); } };  Which pattern is this?

Question 5
Question ID: behavioural-chain-of-responsibility:A5
Paired Question ID: behavioural-chain-of-responsibility:B5
Competency ID: cor-evaluate
Bloom Level: Evaluate
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Wrappers each hold a “next” and either consume a request or forward it; unlike a wrapper that always augments and delegates, a link may stop the request. Best classified as?

OPTIONS:
A. Chain of Responsibility — handlers conditionally handle or forward, possibly stopping the request
B. Decorator — any chain of wrappers is Decorator
C. Adapter — it converts requests
D. Strategy — it swaps algorithms

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Chain of Responsibility — handlers conditionally handle or forward, possibly stopping the request

RATIONALE:
Conditional handle-or-forward (with the option to stop) is Chain of Responsibility; a Decorator always delegates while adding behaviour.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Behavioural module content

Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
What coupling does Chain of Responsibility reduce?

OPTIONS:
A. Between observer and subject
B. Between leaf and composite
C. Between the request’s sender and the specific receiver, since the sender need not know which handler responds
D. Between abstraction and implementation

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. Between the request’s sender and the specific receiver, since the sender need not know which handler responds

RATIONALE:
The sender is decoupled from the concrete handler that ends up servicing the request.

Question 4
Question ID: behavioural-chain-of-responsibility:analyzing
Assessment Type: In-Module Conceptual Assessment
Source Question Index: 3
Competency ID: chain-identify
Bloom Level: Analyzing
Applicable: YES
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft
QUESTION FORMAT: FOUR-OPTION MCQ

QUESTION:
Study this code:

class Filter {
protected:
  Filter* next = nullptr;
public:
  void chain(Filter* n) { next = n; }
  virtual void process(Packet& p) { if (next) next->process(p); }
};
class DropSpam : public Filter {
  void process(Packet& p) override {
    if (p.isSpam()) return;        // stop here
    else Filter::process(p);       // forward
  }
};

Which pattern is shown?

OPTIONS:
A. Decorator — a wrapper that always augments and delegates
B. Composite — a part-whole tree
C. Observer — a subject notifying dependents
D. Chain of Responsibility — linked handlers that each handle or forward, possibly stopping the request

CORRECT ANSWER: D. Chain of Responsibility — linked handlers that each handle or forward, possibly stopping the request

RATIONALE:
Each handler holds a successor and chooses to consume (stop) or forward the request — Chain of Responsibility, distinct from a Decorator that always delegates.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------

Difficulty: Difficult

QUESTION FORMAT: FOUR-OPTION MCQ

QUESTION:
Each link holds a reference to the next and either stops the request on a condition or calls the next, and the caller never learns which link acted. A teammate calls this Decorator. Is that defensible?

OPTIONS:
A. No — a link that may stop the request and hides the receiver from the sender is Chain of Responsibility, not a Decorator that always delegates while adding behaviour
B. Yes — any chain of wrappers holding a next reference is Decorator
C. Yes — because both forward to a successor
D. No — but only because Decorator requires inheritance

CORRECT ANSWER: A. No — a link that may stop the request and hides the receiver from the sender is Chain of Responsibility, not a Decorator that always delegates while adding behaviour

RATIONALE:
Conditional handle-or-stop with sender/receiver decoupling is Chain of Responsibility; a Decorator always delegates while augmenting, so the label is wrong.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Behavioural module content
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

QUESTION:
What is the intent of the Command pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. Convert interfaces
B. Share intrinsic state
C. Encapsulate a request as an object, so you can parameterise, queue, log, and undo operations
D. Traverse a collection

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. Encapsulate a request as an object, so you can parameterise, queue, log, and undo operations

RATIONALE:
Command reifies a request as an object enabling queuing, logging, and undo. The distractors are Adapter, Flyweight, and Iterator.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Behavioural module content

Question 2
Question ID: behavioural-command:A2
Paired Question ID: behavioural-command:B2
Competency ID: cm-enable
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
What does turning a request into a Command object enable?

OPTIONS:
A. Faster compilation
B. Automatic multithreading
C. Removing the receiver
D. Storing, queuing, and undoing operations uniformly, and decoupling the invoker from the receiver

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. Storing, queuing, and undoing operations uniformly, and decoupling the invoker from the receiver

RATIONALE:
A reified request can be stored, queued, replayed, and undone, and it decouples the invoker from the concrete receiver.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

Reifying actions so they can be stored, replayed, and undone is Command. The distractors notify, swap algorithms, or traverse.

QUESTION:
A design calls receiver methods directly from the UI handler with no request object, yet claims to support undo by re-reading the UI. Is this the Command pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. Yes — any action with undo is Command
B. Yes — direct calls are Command
C. No — Command requires reifying each request as an object (with execute/undo); calling the receiver directly is not Command
D. No — Command forbids an invoker

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. No — Command requires reifying each request as an object (with execute/undo); calling the receiver directly is not Command

RATIONALE:
Command’s essence is making each request a first-class object; direct receiver calls without command objects are not Command.

* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

How does Command decouple the invoker from the receiver?

OPTIONS:

QUESTION:
Study this code:

struct Action { virtual void run() = 0; virtual void revert() = 0; };
struct AddRow : Action {
  Table& t;
  Row r;
  void run() override { t.insert(r); }
  void revert() override { t.remove(r); }
};
class Runner {
  std::vector<Action*> log;
public:
  void perform(Action* a) { a->run(); log.push_back(a); }
};

Which pattern is shown?

OPTIONS:
A. Memento — it snapshots state for restore
B. State — behaviour changes with internal mode
C. Strategy — an interchangeable algorithm
D. Command — requests reified with run/revert and recorded by an invoker

CORRECT ANSWER: D. Command — requests reified with run/revert and recorded by an invoker

RATIONALE:
Reified requests carrying run/revert, performed and recorded by an invoker that never names the receiver, is the Command pattern.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------

Question ID: behavioural-command:evaluating

Applicable: YES
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft
QUESTION FORMAT: FOUR-OPTION MCQ

QUESTION:
A UI handler calls receiver methods directly inline and claims to support undo by re-reading the current widget values afterwards; there are no request objects. Does this implement Command?

OPTIONS:
A. No — Command requires each request to be a first-class object with execute/undo; direct inline calls are not Command
B. Yes — any feature offering undo is Command
C. Yes — calling the receiver directly is the standard Command form
D. No — Command forbids having any invoker at all

CORRECT ANSWER: A. No — Command requires each request to be a first-class object with execute/undo; direct inline calls are not Command

RATIONALE:
Command’s essence is making each request a first-class object; calling the receiver directly without command objects is not the pattern.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Behavioural module content
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

QUESTION:
What is the intent of the Interpreter pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. Convert interfaces
B. Share intrinsic state
C. Given a language, define a representation for its grammar and an interpreter that uses the representation to evaluate sentences
D. Provide a surrogate for access

CORRECT ANSWER:

Interpreter represents a grammar and evaluates sentences in that language. The distractors are Adapter, Flyweight, and Proxy.

Competency ID: in-represent

Difficulty: Moderate

QUESTION:
How are grammar rules typically represented in the Interpreter pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. As a single regex
B. As a global lookup table only
C. As one giant switch statement
D. Each grammar rule becomes a class with an interpret() method; expressions form a tree of these objects

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. Each grammar rule becomes a class with an interpret() method; expressions form a tree of these objects

RATIONALE:
Each rule maps to a class with interpret(); a sentence is an abstract syntax tree of those rule objects.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

A. Interpreter
B. Visitor
C. Strategy
D. Composite

A. Interpreter

RATIONALE:
Representing grammar rules as composable evaluable objects is Interpreter. The distractors add operations, swap algorithms, or only model part-whole trees.

* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

Competency ID: interpreter-represent

Applicable: YES
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft
QUESTION FORMAT: FOUR-OPTION MCQ

QUESTION:
How is a sentence typically represented and evaluated in an Interpreter design?

OPTIONS:
A. As a single regular expression matched once
B. As one large switch statement over the raw text
C. As a tree of rule objects, with evaluation recursing over the tree by calling each node’s interpret
D. As a flat array of characters scanned left to right only

CORRECT ANSWER: C. As a tree of rule objects, with evaluation recursing over the tree by calling each node’s interpret

RATIONALE:
A sentence becomes an abstract syntax tree of rule objects, and interpretation recurses over the tree, each node interpreting its children.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

Question 3
Question ID: behavioural-interpreter:applying

QUESTION FORMAT: FOUR-OPTION MCQ

QUESTION:
Users type small filter expressions such as price > 10 AND inStock that nest arbitrarily, and you must evaluate them by composing rules recursively. Which approach fits?

OPTIONS:
A. Model each operator and operand as a class with an interpret method and compose them into a tree
B. Add a new visitor for each kind of filter without changing the operand classes
C. Pick the evaluation algorithm from a menu at runtime
D. Pass the expression through a chain of handlers until one evaluates it

CORRECT ANSWER: A. Model each operator and operand as a class with an interpret method and compose them into a tree

RATIONALE:
Representing each grammar element as a composable evaluable node and recursing is Interpreter. The distractors describe Visitor, Strategy, and Chain of Responsibility.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

Question ID: behavioural-interpreter:analyzing

QUESTION FORMAT: FOUR-OPTION MCQ

QUESTION:
Study this code:

struct Term { virtual int eval(const Env& e) const = 0; };
struct Const : Term { int n; int eval(const Env&) const override { return n; } };
struct Mul : Term {
  const Term* l; const Term* r;
  int eval(const Env& e) const override { return l->eval(e) * r->eval(e); }
};

Which pattern is shown?

OPTIONS:
A. Composite — a structural part-whole tree with no evaluation semantics
B. Strategy — an interchangeable algorithm
C. Observer — a subject notifying dependents
D. Interpreter — grammar-rule classes with eval composed into an expression tree

CORRECT ANSWER: D. Interpreter — grammar-rule classes with eval composed into an expression tree

RATIONALE:
Grammar-rule classes carrying eval and composed into an expression tree that evaluates a sentence is the Interpreter pattern.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

QUESTION:
What is the intent of the Iterator pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. Provide a way to access elements of a collection sequentially without exposing its underlying representation
B. Convert interfaces
C. Ensure one instance
D. Add behaviour by wrapping

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Provide a way to access elements of a collection sequentially without exposing its underlying representation

RATIONALE:
Iterator gives sequential access while hiding the collection’s internals. The distractors are Adapter, Singleton, and Decorator.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Behavioural module content

Question 2
Question ID: behavioural-iterator:A2
Paired Question ID: behavioural-iterator:B2
Competency ID: it-rationale
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Why use an Iterator instead of exposing the collection’s internal structure?

OPTIONS:
A. It makes the collection a Singleton
B. It lets clients traverse uniformly while the collection keeps its representation private and can change it
C. It converts the element type
D. It deletes the collection after traversal

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. It lets clients traverse uniformly while the collection keeps its representation private and can change it

RATIONALE:
Iterator decouples traversal from representation, so the collection can hide and later change its internals.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

C. Iterator

RATIONALE:
Uniform sequential traversal that hides each collection’s internal storage is Iterator.

Paired Question ID: behavioural-iterator:A2

Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
What responsibility moves into the iterator object?

OPTIONS:
A. Owning all the collection’s data
B. Converting element interfaces
C. Tracking the current traversal position, so multiple independent traversals can coexist
D. Notifying observers of changes

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. Tracking the current traversal position, so multiple independent traversals can coexist

RATIONALE:
The iterator holds the traversal cursor, which allows several simultaneous, independent traversals of one collection.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

Question ID: behavioural-iterator:remembering
Assessment Type: In-Module Conceptual Assessment
Source Question Index: 0
Competency ID: iterator-intent
Bloom Level: Remembering
Applicable: YES
Difficulty: Easy
Validation Status: draft
QUESTION FORMAT: FOUR-OPTION MCQ

QUESTION:
The Iterator pattern lets a client ___.

OPTIONS:
A. walk a collection’s elements one by one without seeing how the collection stores them
B. centralise communication between objects
C. restore an object to a previous state
D. wrap an action so it can be undone

CORRECT ANSWER: A. walk a collection’s elements one by one without seeing how the collection stores them

RATIONALE:
Iterator provides sequential access while hiding the collection’s representation. The distractors describe Mediator, Memento, and Command.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Behavioural module content
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 2
Question ID: behavioural-iterator:understanding
Assessment Type: In-Module Conceptual Assessment
Source Question Index: 1
Competency ID: iterator-rationale
Bloom Level: Understanding
Applicable: YES
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft
QUESTION FORMAT: FOUR-OPTION MCQ

QUESTION:
What is the benefit of moving the traversal cursor into a separate iterator object instead of exposing the collection’s internal array?

OPTIONS:
A. It forces the collection to become a single shared instance
B. It converts each element to a new type during the walk
C. It lets the collection notify dependents on every step
D. The collection can keep its storage private and even change it, while clients still traverse uniformly

CORRECT ANSWER: D. The collection can keep its storage private and even change it, while clients still traverse uniformly

RATIONALE:
A separate cursor decouples traversal from representation, so the collection hides and can later change its internals while clients traverse uniformly.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 3
Question ID: behavioural-iterator:applying
Assessment Type: In-Module Conceptual Assessment
Source Question Index: 2
Competency ID: iterator-select
Bloom Level: Applying
Applicable: YES
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft
QUESTION FORMAT: FOUR-OPTION MCQ

QUESTION:
A library exposes a ring buffer and a skip list; users should loop over either with identical code and even run two independent passes over the same container at once, without knowing the storage. Which approach fits?

OPTIONS:
A. Give each container a hub that coordinates who reads next
B. Snapshot the container before each read so it can be rolled back
C. Hand out cursor objects that track position and expose a uniform advance/done interface
D. Add a visitor that defines a new operation per element type

CORRECT ANSWER: C. Hand out cursor objects that track position and expose a uniform advance/done interface

RATIONALE:
Uniform sequential access via position-tracking cursors that hide storage and allow concurrent passes is Iterator. The distractors describe Mediator, Memento, and Visitor.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 4
Question ID: behavioural-iterator:analyzing
Assessment Type: In-Module Conceptual Assessment
Source Question Index: 3
Competency ID: iterator-identify
Bloom Level: Analyzing
Applicable: YES
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft
QUESTION FORMAT: FOUR-OPTION MCQ

QUESTION:
Study this code:

class RangeCursor {
  int current;
  int stop;
public:
  RangeCursor(int begin, int end) : current(begin), stop(end) {}
  bool done() const { return current >= stop; }
  int value() const { return current; }
  void advance() { ++current; }
};

Which pattern does RangeCursor embody?

OPTIONS:
A. Strategy — an interchangeable algorithm
B. State — behaviour driven by internal mode
C. Command — a reified request
D. Iterator — an object that tracks position and exposes done/value/advance

CORRECT ANSWER: D. Iterator — an object that tracks position and exposes done/value/advance

RATIONALE:
An object that holds the traversal position and exposes done/value/advance, hiding the underlying sequence, is an Iterator.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 5
Question ID: behavioural-iterator:evaluating
Assessment Type: In-Module Conceptual Assessment
Source Question Index: 4
Competency ID: iterator-evaluate
Bloom Level: Evaluating
Applicable: YES
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft
QUESTION FORMAT: FOUR-OPTION MCQ

QUESTION:
A so-called traversal API requires callers to read the collection’s public head pointer and node links directly, chasing them manually to loop. Does this honour the Iterator pattern’s intent?

OPTIONS:
A. No — exposing the internal representation to callers defeats the encapsulation Iterator exists to provide
B. Yes — manual pointer chasing is the canonical Iterator
C. Yes — any loop over elements counts as Iterator
D. No — Iterator prohibits sequential access entirely

CORRECT ANSWER: A. No — exposing the internal representation to callers defeats the encapsulation Iterator exists to provide

RATIONALE:
Iterator exists precisely to hide the representation; forcing callers to use internal links breaks the encapsulation it is meant to preserve.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Behavioural module content
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

================================================================Question 1
Question ID: behavioural-mediator:A1
Paired Question ID: behavioural-mediator:B1
Competency ID: md-intent
Bloom Level: Remember
Difficulty: Easy
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
What is the intent of the Mediator pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. Convert interfaces
B. Traverse a collection
C. Define an object that centralises and coordinates communication between a set of objects so they don’t refer to each other directly
D. Share intrinsic state

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. Define an object that centralises and coordinates communication between a set of objects so they don’t refer to each other directly

RATIONALE:
Mediator centralises inter-object communication. The distractors are Adapter, Iterator, and Flyweight.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Behavioural module content

------------------------------------------------
Question 2
Question ID: behavioural-mediator:A2
Paired Question ID: behavioural-mediator:B2
Competency ID: md-coupling
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
How does a Mediator reduce coupling among colleague objects?

OPTIONS:
A. By merging all colleagues into one class
B. By making each colleague a Singleton
C. By converting their interfaces
D. Colleagues talk only to the mediator, which routes interactions, instead of holding references to each other

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. Colleagues talk only to the mediator, which routes interactions, instead of holding references to each other

RATIONALE:
Colleagues communicate through the mediator rather than referencing each other, cutting many-to-many coupling.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 3
Question ID: behavioural-mediator:A3
Paired Question ID: behavioural-mediator:B3
Competency ID: md-select
Bloom Level: Apply
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A dialog’s many widgets (list, text box, button) must enable/disable each other in complex ways; you want to avoid each widget referencing all the others. Which pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. Mediator
B. Observer
C. Composite
D. Command

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Mediator

RATIONALE:
Centralising the widgets’ mutual interactions in one coordinator is Mediator. Observer would be one-way notification.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------

Question ID: behavioural-mediator:A4
Paired Question ID: behavioural-mediator:B4
Competency ID: md-identify
Bloom Level: Analyze
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Examine:  class Mediator { public: virtual void notify(Widget* sender, std::string ev) = 0; };  class Dialog : public Mediator { Button* ok; ListBox* list; public: void notify(Widget* s, std::string ev) override { if (s == list && ev == "select") ok->enable(); } };  Which pattern is this?

OPTIONS:
A. Observer
B. Mediator
C. Facade
D. Visitor

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. Mediator

RATIONALE:
A hub that receives events from colleagues and coordinates how others respond is Mediator.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

Paired Question ID: behavioural-mediator:B5

Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A hub receives events from peer objects and tells other peers how to react, governing their mutual interaction (not one-way notification, not just simplifying a subsystem). Best classified as?

OPTIONS:
A. Observer — it is one-to-many notification
B. Facade — it just simplifies a subsystem
C. Mediator — it centralises and coordinates mutual communication among peers
D. Adapter — it converts interfaces

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. Mediator — it centralises and coordinates mutual communication among peers

RATIONALE:
Two-way coordination of peers’ mutual interactions is Mediator; Observer is one-way, Facade is one-way subsystem simplification.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Behavioural module content

Question 3

QUESTION FORMAT: FOUR-OPTION MCQ

QUESTION:
In a booking form, selecting a country must refilter the city list, toggle a postcode field, and enable the submit button; you want to avoid each control holding references to all the others. Which approach fits?

OPTIONS:
A. Route every control’s events through one coordinator that decides how the others react
B. Have each control notify a list of dependents and update themselves
C. Pass each event through a chain of handlers until one acts
D. Give each control an interchangeable algorithm chosen at runtime

CORRECT ANSWER: A. Route every control’s events through one coordinator that decides how the others react

RATIONALE:
Centralising the controls’ mutual interactions in one coordinator is Mediator; one-way notification would be Observer.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

Question 4
Question ID: behavioural-mediator:analyzing
Assessment Type: In-Module Conceptual Assessment
Source Question Index: 3
Competency ID: mediator-identify
Bloom Level: Analyzing
Applicable: YES
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft
QUESTION FORMAT: FOUR-OPTION MCQ

QUESTION:
Study this code:

class Tower;
class Plane {
  Tower* tower;
public:
  void requestLanding() { /* tower->coordinate(this) */ }
};
class Tower {
  std::vector<Plane*> planes;
public:
  void coordinate(Plane* requester) {
    /* decide order, tell other planes to hold */
  }
};

Planes interact only through Tower. Which pattern is shown?

OPTIONS:
A. Observer — a subject broadcasting to dependents
B. Mediator — a hub coordinating the mutual interaction of otherwise-decoupled peers
C. Facade — a simplified entry point to a subsystem
D. Visitor — operations added by double dispatch

CORRECT ANSWER: B. Mediator — a hub coordinating the mutual interaction of otherwise-decoupled peers

RATIONALE:
Peers (planes) that never reference each other and interact only through a central coordinator that governs their mutual behaviour is Mediator.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------

QUESTION:
What is the intent of the Memento pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. Capture and externalise an object’s internal state so it can be restored later, without violating encapsulation
B. Convert interfaces
C. Ensure one instance
D. Share intrinsic state across objects

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Capture and externalise an object’s internal state so it can be restored later, without violating encapsulation

RATIONALE:
Memento captures and restores state without breaking encapsulation. The distractors are Adapter, Singleton, and Flyweight.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Behavioural module content

------------------------------------------------
Question 2
Question ID: behavioural-memento:A2
Paired Question ID: behavioural-memento:B2
Competency ID: mm-encaps
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
How does Memento preserve encapsulation while saving state?

OPTIONS:
A. It makes all fields public
B. The originator creates an opaque memento that the caretaker stores without inspecting its internals
C. It copies the originator’s class definition
D. It exposes the state through getters to everyone

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. The originator creates an opaque memento that the caretaker stores without inspecting its internals

RATIONALE:
The originator builds the memento; the caretaker stores it opaquely, never reading the internals, so encapsulation holds.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------

QUESTION:
A drawing app needs unlimited undo by snapshotting the canvas state and restoring it later, without exposing the canvas’s private fields. Which pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. Command
B. State
C. Memento
D. Observer

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. Memento

RATIONALE:
Snapshotting and restoring state without exposing internals is Memento. Command reifies requests; State models modes.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

A. Command

QUESTION:
A “snapshot” design exposes all of an object’s internal fields publicly so external code can copy and later write them back. Does this satisfy Memento’s intent?

OPTIONS:
A. No — Memento must capture state without breaking encapsulation; exposing internals publicly violates that
B. Yes — public fields are required for Memento
C. Yes — any save/restore is Memento
D. No — Memento cannot restore state

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. No — Memento must capture state without breaking encapsulation; exposing internals publicly violates that

RATIONALE:
Memento’s point is capturing state without breaking encapsulation; publicly exposing internals defeats it.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Behavioural module content

Question 2
Question ID: behavioural-memento:B2
Paired Question ID: behavioural-memento:A2
Competency ID: mm-encaps
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
What are the three roles in the Memento pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. Subject, Observer, Client
B. Context, State, Transition
C. Originator (owns state), Memento (stores a snapshot), and Caretaker (keeps mementos without inspecting them)
D. Abstraction, Implementor, Bridge

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. Originator (owns state), Memento (stores a snapshot), and Caretaker (keeps mementos without inspecting them)

RATIONALE:
Memento’s roles are Originator, Memento, and Caretaker; the caretaker stores snapshots opaquely.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------

QUESTION:
A game must let players save a checkpoint and reload exactly that state later, while keeping the player object’s fields private. Which pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. Command
B. Prototype
C. State
D. Memento

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. Memento

RATIONALE:
Saving and restoring an object’s state without exposing its fields is Memento.

* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

Applicable: YES
Difficulty: Moderate

QUESTION FORMAT: FOUR-OPTION MCQ

QUESTION:
How does the Memento pattern preserve encapsulation while saving an object’s state?

OPTIONS:
A. It makes all of the object’s fields public so anyone can copy them
B. The originator produces an opaque memento that the caretaker stores without reading its contents
C. It exposes the state through getters available to every caller
D. It copies the originator’s class definition at runtime

CORRECT ANSWER: B. The originator produces an opaque memento that the caretaker stores without reading its contents

RATIONALE:
The originator builds the memento and the caretaker holds it opaquely, never inspecting the internals, so encapsulation is preserved.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

Question 3

QUESTION:
A text editor must support unlimited undo by saving the buffer’s state at each step and restoring an earlier one on demand, without exposing the buffer’s private fields. Which approach fits?

OPTIONS:
A. Have the buffer return an opaque snapshot a history keeper stores and hands back to restore it
B. Wrap each edit as a command and replay it in reverse
C. Give the buffer a current-state object that changes its behaviour
D. Notify a list of dependents whenever the buffer changes

CORRECT ANSWER: A. Have the buffer return an opaque snapshot a history keeper stores and hands back to restore it

RATIONALE:
Producing an opaque snapshot stored by a caretaker and used to restore the object, without exposing internals, is Memento. The distractors lean toward Command, State, and Observer.

Question 4
Question ID: behavioural-memento:analyzing
Assessment Type: In-Module Conceptual Assessment
Source Question Index: 3
Competency ID: memento-identify
Bloom Level: Analyzing
Applicable: YES
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft
QUESTION FORMAT: FOUR-OPTION MCQ

QUESTION:
Study this code:

class Canvas {
  std::vector<Stroke> strokes;
public:
  class Snapshot {
    friend class Canvas;
    std::vector<Stroke> saved;
    explicit Snapshot(std::vector<Stroke> s) : saved(std::move(s)) {}
  };
  Snapshot capture() const { return Snapshot(strokes); }
  void restore(const Snapshot& s) { strokes = s.saved; }
};

Which pattern is shown?

OPTIONS:
A. Command — a reified request with execute/undo
B. State — behaviour changes with internal mode
C. Prototype — cloning via a virtual copy
D. Memento — an originator producing/consuming an opaque state snapshot only it can read

CORRECT ANSWER: D. Memento — an originator producing/consuming an opaque state snapshot only it can read

RATIONALE:
An originator emitting an opaque snapshot (whose internals only it can read via friendship) and restoring from it is the Memento pattern.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

Question 5
Question ID: behavioural-memento:evaluating
Assessment Type: In-Module Conceptual Assessment
Source Question Index: 4

Bloom Level: Evaluating

Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft
QUESTION FORMAT: FOUR-OPTION MCQ

QUESTION:
A so-called snapshot design makes every internal field of an object public so external code can read them all, copy them, and later write them back directly. Does this honour Memento’s intent?

OPTIONS:
A. No — Memento captures state without breaking encapsulation; exposing all internals publicly violates that core intent
B. Yes — public fields are a requirement of Memento
C. Yes — any save-and-restore scheme is Memento
D. No — because Memento is unable to restore state at all

CORRECT ANSWER: A. No — Memento captures state without breaking encapsulation; exposing all internals publicly violates that core intent

RATIONALE:
Memento exists to capture state while keeping internals hidden; making every field public defeats the encapsulation the pattern is meant to protect.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Behavioural module content
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

Difficulty: Easy
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
What is the intent of the Observer pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. Convert interfaces
B. Share intrinsic state
C. Define a one-to-many dependency so that when one object changes state, its dependents are notified automatically
D. Encapsulate a request as an object

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. Define a one-to-many dependency so that when one object changes state, its dependents are notified automatically

RATIONALE:
Observer establishes one-to-many automatic notification. The distractors are Adapter, Flyweight, and Command.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Behavioural module content

------------------------------------------------
Question 2
Question ID: behavioural-observer:A2
Paired Question ID: behavioural-observer:B2
Competency ID: ob-mechanism
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
How do observers learn about changes in the subject?

OPTIONS:
A. Observers poll a global variable
B. Observers inherit the subject’s data
C. Each observer edits the subject directly
D. The subject holds a list of observers and calls their update method when its state changes

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. The subject holds a list of observers and calls their update method when its state changes

RATIONALE:
The subject keeps registered observers and notifies them (calls update) on change; observers do not poll or inherit.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------

OPTIONS:
A. Observer
B. Mediator
C. Command
D. Strategy

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Observer

RATIONALE:
Automatic one-to-many notification with loose coupling to dependents is Observer. The distractors coordinate peers, encapsulate requests, or swap algorithms.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

Why does the subject depend only on an observer interface, not concrete observers?

OPTIONS:
A. So new observer types can subscribe without modifying the subject (loose coupling)
B. So the subject can delete observers’ data
C. Because observers must be Singletons
D. So the subject can convert their interfaces

RATIONALE:

When a sensor reading updates, a live graph, an audit log, and a threshold alarm must all react immediately, the sensor must not know their concrete types, and new reactors may be added later. Which approach fits?

Question 4
Question ID: behavioural-observer:analyzing

QUESTION FORMAT: FOUR-OPTION MCQ

QUESTION:
Study this code:

struct Watcher { virtual void onChange(double temp) = 0; };
class Thermostat {
  std::vector<Watcher*> watchers;
  double temp = 0;
public:
  void subscribe(Watcher* w) { watchers.push_back(w); }
  void setTemp(double t) { temp = t; for (auto* w : watchers) w->onChange(temp); }
};

Which pattern describes how Thermostat collaborates with its Watchers?

OPTIONS:
A. Mediator — a hub coordinates mutual interaction
B. Visitor — operations are added via double dispatch
C. Observer — the subject broadcasts state changes to registered dependents
D. Iterator — it sequentially accesses elements

CORRECT ANSWER: C. Observer — the subject broadcasts state changes to registered dependents

RATIONALE:
Thermostat keeps a list of dependents and broadcasts each state change to all of them — the one-to-many notification of Observer.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

What is the intent of the State pattern?

Question 2

Difficulty: Moderate

QUESTION:
How does the State pattern avoid large conditional blocks on a status field?

OPTIONS:
A. By using one giant switch statement
B. By making the object a Singleton
C. By copying the object per state
D. By representing each state as an object and delegating behaviour to the current state, which can switch to another

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. By representing each state as an object and delegating behaviour to the current state, which can switch to another

RATIONALE:
Each state becomes an object encapsulating its behaviour and transitions; the context delegates to the current one.

* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

A. State
