Behaviour and transitions driven by the object’s changing mode is State. Strategy is client-chosen and not transition-driven.

QUESTION:
Examine:  class State { public: virtual void insertCoin(Machine&) = 0; };  class NoCoin : public State { void insertCoin(Machine& m) override { m.setState(new HasCoin()); } };  class Machine { State* s; public: void setState(State* x) { s = x; } void insertCoin() { s->insertCoin(*this); } };  Which pattern is this?

OPTIONS:
A. Strategy
B. State
C. Command
D. Mediator

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. State

RATIONALE:
Behaviour delegated to a current-state object that itself triggers the transition to another state is the State pattern.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

QUESTION:
How does the State pattern replace a sprawling switch on a status field?

OPTIONS:
A. By making the object a single shared instance
B. By copying the object once per status value
C. By representing each state as an object the host delegates to, with states able to switch the host to another state

QUESTION:
Study this code:

class Door;
struct DoorState { virtual void push(Door& d) = 0; };
struct Closed : DoorState { void push(Door& d) override; };
class Door {
  DoorState* state;
public:
  void setState(DoorState* s) { state = s; }
  void push() { state->push(*this); }
};
// Closed::push sets the door to an Open state

Which pattern does Door’s collaboration with DoorState demonstrate?

OPTIONS:
A. Strategy — an injected algorithm chosen by the caller
B. Observer — it notifies dependents
C. Command — a reified request
D. State — behaviour is delegated to a current-state object that performs the transition

CORRECT ANSWER: D. State — behaviour is delegated to a current-state object that performs the transition

RATIONALE:
The host delegates to a current-state object that itself triggers the move to the next state — the hallmark of State, not caller-chosen Strategy.

QUESTION:
How does Strategy let the algorithm change without changing the client?

OPTIONS:
A. The client edits the algorithm’s code each time
B. The client depends on a strategy interface and delegates, so concrete algorithms swap freely
C. The client inherits each algorithm
D. The algorithms share one global instance

B. The client depends on a strategy interface and delegates, so concrete algorithms swap freely

The client holds a strategy interface and delegates; concrete strategies are interchangeable behind it.

B. Context and strategy are the same object

Question 4
Question ID: behavioural-strategy:analyzing

QUESTION FORMAT: FOUR-OPTION MCQ

QUESTION:
Study this code:

struct TaxRule { virtual double apply(double amount) const = 0; };
struct FlatTax : TaxRule { double apply(double a) const override { return a * 0.2; } };
class Invoice {
  const TaxRule* rule;
public:
  explicit Invoice(const TaxRule* r) : rule(r) {}
  double total(double net) const { return net + rule->apply(net); }
};

Which pattern does Invoice’s collaboration with TaxRule demonstrate?

OPTIONS:
A. State — the rule changes the object’s mode
B. Template Method — a fixed skeleton with overridable steps
C. Command — the request is reified for undo
D. Strategy — the host delegates to an injected, interchangeable algorithm

CORRECT ANSWER: D. Strategy — the host delegates to an injected, interchangeable algorithm

RATIONALE:
Invoice delegates the varying calculation to an injected TaxRule it never names concretely, with no state-transition methods — that is Strategy.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

================================================================Question 1
Question ID: behavioural-template-method:A1
Paired Question ID: behavioural-template-method:B1
Competency ID: tm-intent
Bloom Level: Remember
Difficulty: Easy
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
What is the intent of the Template Method pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. Define the skeleton of an algorithm in a base method, deferring some steps to subclasses
B. Convert interfaces
C. Ensure one instance
D. Share intrinsic state

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Define the skeleton of an algorithm in a base method, deferring some steps to subclasses

RATIONALE:
Template Method fixes an algorithm’s skeleton and lets subclasses supply steps. The distractors are Adapter, Singleton, and Flyweight.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Behavioural module content

------------------------------------------------
Question 2
Question ID: behavioural-template-method:A2
Paired Question ID: behavioural-template-method:B2
Competency ID: tm-fixedvary
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
In Template Method, what stays fixed and what varies?

OPTIONS:
A. Everything is overridden
B. The overall step sequence is fixed in the base class while specific steps are overridden by subclasses
C. Nothing can be overridden
D. The client supplies the whole algorithm at runtime

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. The overall step sequence is fixed in the base class while specific steps are overridden by subclasses

RATIONALE:
The invariant sequence lives in the base method; subclasses override only the designated variable steps.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 3
Question ID: behavioural-template-method:A3
Paired Question ID: behavioural-template-method:B3
Competency ID: tm-select
Bloom Level: Apply
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Several report generators share the same overall flow (open → fetch → format → save) but differ only in how they format. You want the flow defined once. Which pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. Strategy
B. Command
C. Template Method
D. Observer

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. Template Method

RATIONALE:
A fixed algorithm skeleton with a varying step implemented per subclass is Template Method. Strategy would inject the whole algorithm as an object.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 4
Question ID: behavioural-template-method:A4
Paired Question ID: behavioural-template-method:B4
Competency ID: tm-identify
Bloom Level: Analyze
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Examine:  class Game { public: void play() { start(); while (!over()) turn(); end(); } protected: virtual void start() = 0; virtual void turn() = 0; virtual bool over() = 0; virtual void end() = 0; };  class Chess : public Game { /* overrides */ };  Which pattern is this?

OPTIONS:
A. Strategy
B. State
C. Command
D. Template Method

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. Template Method

RATIONALE:
A non-virtual base method fixing the flow and calling overridable steps is Template Method.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 5
Question ID: behavioural-template-method:A5
Paired Question ID: behavioural-template-method:B5
Competency ID: tm-evaluate
Bloom Level: Evaluate
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
An algorithm’s invariant steps live in a base method that calls overridable hooks; subclasses customise only those hooks. Is this Template Method or Strategy?

OPTIONS:
A. Template Method — variation happens via subclass overrides of fixed hook steps (inheritance), not a swapped strategy object
B. Strategy — any variation is Strategy
C. Observer — it notifies dependents
D. Composite — it forms a tree

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Template Method — variation happens via subclass overrides of fixed hook steps (inheritance), not a swapped strategy object

RATIONALE:
Template Method varies behaviour through subclass overrides of fixed hooks (inheritance); Strategy varies it by composing a swappable object.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Behavioural module content

Paired Question ID: behavioural-template-method:A1

Bloom Level: Remember
Difficulty: Easy
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Template Method defines ___.

OPTIONS:
A. a one-to-many dependency
B. an algorithm’s skeleton whose selected steps subclasses fill in without changing its structure
C. a surrogate for access control
D. a part-whole hierarchy

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. an algorithm’s skeleton whose selected steps subclasses fill in without changing its structure

RATIONALE:
Template Method defines an algorithm skeleton with subclass-filled steps. The distractors are Observer, Proxy, and Composite.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Behavioural module content

------------------------------------------------
Question 2
Question ID: behavioural-template-method:B2
Paired Question ID: behavioural-template-method:A2
Competency ID: tm-fixedvary
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Where is the invariant control flow located in Template Method?

OPTIONS:
A. In each subclass separately
B. In a separate strategy object
C. In a base-class method that calls the overridable primitive operations in a fixed order
D. In the client code

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. In a base-class method that calls the overridable primitive operations in a fixed order

RATIONALE:
The base class owns the fixed control flow and calls the overridable steps in order.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------

QUESTION:
Data importers for CSV, JSON, and XML all follow validate → parse → transform → persist, differing only in parsing. You want to write the sequence once. Which pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. Visitor
B. Mediator
C. Bridge
D. Template Method

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. Template Method

RATIONALE:
A shared fixed sequence with one varying step per subclass is Template Method.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

Question 4

QUESTION:
Examine:  class Beverage { public: void make() { boil(); brew(); pour(); addExtras(); } protected: void boil(); void pour(); virtual void brew() = 0; virtual void addExtras() = 0; };  class Tea : public Beverage { /* overrides brew, addExtras */ };  Which pattern is this?

OPTIONS:
A. Template Method
B. Strategy
C. Decorator
D. Command

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Template Method

RATIONALE:
A fixed make() sequence calling some concrete and some overridable steps is Template Method.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

Question 5
Question ID: behavioural-template-method:B5
Paired Question ID: behavioural-template-method:A5

Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A base class fixes the sequence run() { setUp(); execute(); tearDown(); } where execute() is abstract and subclasses implement it. Is “Template Method” defensible?

OPTIONS:
A. No — this is Strategy
B. Yes — a fixed skeleton with subclass-supplied steps via overriding is exactly Template Method
C. No — Template Method forbids abstract steps
D. Yes — but only with one subclass

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. Yes — a fixed skeleton with subclass-supplied steps via overriding is exactly Template Method

RATIONALE:
A fixed skeleton whose variable steps are supplied by subclass overrides is Template Method.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

QUESTION:
The Template Method pattern keeps an algorithm’s ___ fixed in a base method while letting subclasses supply certain steps.

OPTIONS:
A. list of observers
B. overall step sequence
C. state-transition table
D. request history

CORRECT ANSWER: B. overall step sequence

RATIONALE:
Template Method fixes the overall step sequence in the base class and defers chosen steps to subclasses. The distractors belong to Observer, State, and Command.

Question 2
Question ID: behavioural-template-method:understanding
Assessment Type: In-Module Conceptual Assessment
Source Question Index: 1
Competency ID: template-fixedvary
Bloom Level: Understanding
Applicable: YES
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft
QUESTION FORMAT: FOUR-OPTION MCQ

QUESTION:
In a Template Method design, what is fixed and what varies?

OPTIONS:
A. Everything is overridden by each subclass
B. The base method fixes the order of steps while subclasses override only the designated variable steps
C. Nothing in the algorithm can ever be overridden
D. The caller injects the entire algorithm as a separate object

CORRECT ANSWER: B. The base method fixes the order of steps while subclasses override only the designated variable steps

RATIONALE:
The invariant control flow lives in the base method; subclasses override only the marked variable steps, not the sequence.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 3
Question ID: behavioural-template-method:applying
Assessment Type: In-Module Conceptual Assessment
Source Question Index: 2
Competency ID: template-select
Bloom Level: Applying
Applicable: YES
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft
QUESTION FORMAT: FOUR-OPTION MCQ

QUESTION:
Three exporters share the flow open → gather → render → close and differ only in how they render, and you want that flow written exactly once. Which approach fits?

OPTIONS:
A. Inject the whole export algorithm as a swappable object the caller chooses
B. Put the fixed flow in a base method that calls an overridable render step subclasses implement
C. Pass the export request through a chain of handlers
D. Coordinate the exporters through a central hub

CORRECT ANSWER: B. Put the fixed flow in a base method that calls an overridable render step subclasses implement

RATIONALE:
A fixed skeleton in a base method calling an overridable step per subclass is Template Method; injecting the whole algorithm would be Strategy.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

Question 4
Question ID: behavioural-template-method:analyzing
Assessment Type: In-Module Conceptual Assessment
Source Question Index: 3
Competency ID: template-identify
Bloom Level: Analyzing
Applicable: YES
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft
QUESTION FORMAT: FOUR-OPTION MCQ

QUESTION:
Study this code:

class Report {
public:
  void build() { header(); body(); footer(); }
protected:
  void header() { /* fixed */ }
  void footer() { /* fixed */ }
  virtual void body() = 0;
};
class SalesReport : public Report {
  void body() override { /* sales rows */ }
};

Which pattern does build() demonstrate?

OPTIONS:
A. Strategy — the body is an injected algorithm object
B. State — build changes with internal mode
C. Template Method — a fixed sequence calling an overridable hook step
D. Command — build reifies a request

CORRECT ANSWER: C. Template Method — a fixed sequence calling an overridable hook step

RATIONALE:
A non-overridden base method fixing the order and calling an overridable hook (body) implemented by subclasses is Template Method.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 5
Question ID: behavioural-template-method:evaluating
Assessment Type: In-Module Conceptual Assessment
Source Question Index: 4
Competency ID: template-evaluate
Bloom Level: Evaluating
Applicable: YES
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft
QUESTION FORMAT: FOUR-OPTION MCQ

QUESTION:
An algorithm’s invariant steps live in a base method that invokes overridable hooks, and subclasses customise behaviour only by overriding those hooks via inheritance. Is this better called Template Method or Strategy?

OPTIONS:
A. Strategy — any variation in behaviour is Strategy
B. Observer — the hooks notify dependents
C. Composite — the steps form a tree
D. Template Method — variation comes from subclass overrides of fixed hook steps, not a composed swappable object

CORRECT ANSWER: D. Template Method — variation comes from subclass overrides of fixed hook steps, not a composed swappable object

RATIONALE:
Template Method varies behaviour through inheritance and subclass overrides of fixed hooks; Strategy varies it by composing a swappable algorithm object.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Behavioural module content
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

================================================================Question 1
Question ID: behavioural-visitor:A1
Paired Question ID: behavioural-visitor:B1
Competency ID: vs-intent
Bloom Level: Remember
Difficulty: Easy
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
What is the intent of the Visitor pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. Represent an operation to perform on the elements of an object structure, letting you add new operations without changing the element classes
B. Convert interfaces
C. Ensure one instance
D. Traverse a collection sequentially

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Represent an operation to perform on the elements of an object structure, letting you add new operations without changing the element classes

RATIONALE:
Visitor adds operations over a structure without modifying its element classes. The distractors are Adapter, Singleton, and Iterator.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Behavioural module content

------------------------------------------------
Question 2
Question ID: behavioural-visitor:A2
Paired Question ID: behavioural-visitor:B2
Competency ID: vs-mechanism
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
How does Visitor add a new operation across a class hierarchy without editing those classes?

OPTIONS:
A. By rewriting every element class
B. Each element accepts a visitor and dispatches to the visitor’s method for its type; new operations are new visitor classes
C. By making each element a Singleton
D. By converting each element’s interface

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. Each element accepts a visitor and dispatches to the visitor’s method for its type; new operations are new visitor classes

RATIONALE:
Elements accept() a visitor and call back the type-specific visit() (double dispatch); a new operation is simply a new visitor.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 3
Question ID: behavioural-visitor:A3
Paired Question ID: behavioural-visitor:B3
Competency ID: vs-select
Bloom Level: Apply
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
An AST has many node types; you must add operations like type-check, pretty-print, and optimise without modifying each node class every time. Which pattern?

OPTIONS:
A. Composite
B. Iterator
C. Visitor
D. Strategy

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. Visitor

RATIONALE:
Adding many operations over a stable element hierarchy without editing elements is Visitor.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 4
Question ID: behavioural-visitor:A4
Paired Question ID: behavioural-visitor:B4
Competency ID: vs-identify
Bloom Level: Analyze
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Examine:  class Visitor; class Node { public: virtual void accept(Visitor&) = 0; };  class Number : public Node { public: int v; void accept(Visitor& vi) override; /* vi.visit(*this) */ };  class Visitor { public: virtual void visit(Number&) = 0; virtual void visit(Add&) = 0; };  Which pattern is this?

OPTIONS:
A. Composite
B. Mediator
C. Observer
D. Visitor

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. Visitor

RATIONALE:
accept()/visit() double dispatch over an element hierarchy is the Visitor pattern.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 5
Question ID: behavioural-visitor:A5
Paired Question ID: behavioural-visitor:B5
Competency ID: vs-evaluate
Bloom Level: Evaluate
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A team needs to add many unrelated operations over a stable set of node types and rarely adds new node types. Is Visitor an appropriate choice, and why?

OPTIONS:
A. Yes — Visitor fits stable element types with frequently-added operations, localising each operation in a visitor (though adding new element types is costly)
B. No — Visitor is for adding new element types easily
C. No — Visitor cannot support multiple operations
D. Yes — because Visitor makes adding new node types trivial

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Yes — Visitor fits stable element types with frequently-added operations, localising each operation in a visitor (though adding new element types is costly)

RATIONALE:
Visitor suits stable element hierarchies with many added operations; its known cost is that new element types require touching every visitor.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Behavioural module content

Question 2
Question ID: behavioural-visitor:B2
Paired Question ID: behavioural-visitor:A2
Competency ID: vs-mechanism
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
What technique lets the correct visitor method run for each concrete element type?

OPTIONS:
A. A global switch on a type tag
B. Reflection at link time
C. Double dispatch — accept() on the element calls the visitor overload specific to that element’s type
D. Copying the element first

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. Double dispatch — accept() on the element calls the visitor overload specific to that element’s type

RATIONALE:
Double dispatch (element.accept → visitor.visit overload) selects the right operation for the concrete element type.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

Question 5

Bloom Level: Evaluate
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Adding a new node type to a working Visitor design forces edits to every visitor class. Is this a known and acceptable trade-off of Visitor?

OPTIONS:
A. No — Visitor makes adding element types free
B. Yes — Visitor trades cheap new operations for costly new element types; this is its well-known trade-off, acceptable when element types are stable
C. No — it means the design is wrong
D. Yes — but only in C++

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. Yes — Visitor trades cheap new operations for costly new element types; this is its well-known trade-off, acceptable when element types are stable

RATIONALE:
Visitor’s recognised trade-off: easy new operations, costly new element types — acceptable when the element set is stable.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns
* CodiNeo Behavioural module content

Question 2
Question ID: behavioural-visitor:understanding
Assessment Type: In-Module Conceptual Assessment
Source Question Index: 1
Competency ID: visitor-mechanism
Bloom Level: Understanding
Applicable: YES
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft
QUESTION FORMAT: FOUR-OPTION MCQ

QUESTION:
What technique lets the correct operation run for each concrete element type in a Visitor design?

OPTIONS:
A. A global switch over an integer type tag
B. Reflection performed at link time
C. Double dispatch — each element’s accept calls back the visitor overload matching the element’s concrete type
D. Copying each element before operating on it

CORRECT ANSWER: C. Double dispatch — each element’s accept calls back the visitor overload matching the element’s concrete type

RATIONALE:
Double dispatch (element.accept → the visitor’s type-specific visit) selects the right operation for each concrete element type; a new operation is just a new visitor.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 3
Question ID: behavioural-visitor:applying
Assessment Type: In-Module Conceptual Assessment
Source Question Index: 2
Competency ID: visitor-select
Bloom Level: Applying
Applicable: YES
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft
QUESTION FORMAT: FOUR-OPTION MCQ

QUESTION:
A document model has paragraph, image, and table nodes; you must keep adding operations like word-count, export-to-HTML, and spell-check without editing each node class for every new operation. Which approach fits?

OPTIONS:
A. Give each node a current-state object that changes its behaviour
B. Move each operation into a separate visitor with one visit method per node type, with nodes calling back via accept
C. Pass each node through a chain of handlers
D. Coordinate the nodes through a central hub

CORRECT ANSWER: B. Move each operation into a separate visitor with one visit method per node type, with nodes calling back via accept

RATIONALE:
Adding many operations over a stable element hierarchy without editing the elements, via accept/visit double dispatch, is Visitor. The distractors describe State, Chain of Responsibility, and Mediator.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 4
Question ID: behavioural-visitor:analyzing
Assessment Type: In-Module Conceptual Assessment
Source Question Index: 3
Competency ID: visitor-identify
Bloom Level: Analyzing
Applicable: YES
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft
QUESTION FORMAT: FOUR-OPTION MCQ

QUESTION:
Study this code:

struct Circle; struct Rect;
struct ShapeOp {
  virtual void on(Circle&) = 0;
  virtual void on(Rect&) = 0;
};
struct Shape { virtual void accept(ShapeOp&) = 0; };
struct Circle : Shape { void accept(ShapeOp& op) override { op.on(*this); } };

Which pattern does the accept/on collaboration demonstrate?

OPTIONS:
A. Composite — a part-whole tree
B. Mediator — peers coordinated by a hub
C. Observer — a subject notifying dependents
D. Visitor — accept/visit double dispatch adding operations over an element hierarchy

CORRECT ANSWER: D. Visitor — accept/visit double dispatch adding operations over an element hierarchy

RATIONALE:
Elements exposing accept and calling back a type-specific operation (double dispatch) so operations live in the operation object is Visitor.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

------------------------------------------------
Question 5
Question ID: behavioural-visitor:evaluating
Assessment Type: In-Module Conceptual Assessment
Source Question Index: 4
Competency ID: visitor-evaluate
Bloom Level: Evaluating
Applicable: YES
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft
QUESTION FORMAT: FOUR-OPTION MCQ

QUESTION:
A team must add many unrelated operations over a node hierarchy whose set of node types almost never changes. Is Visitor an appropriate choice, and why?

OPTIONS:
A. Yes — Visitor suits a stable element set with frequently-added operations, isolating each operation in its own visitor (accepting that new element types would be costly)
B. No — Visitor is meant for adding new element types cheaply
C. No — Visitor can express only a single operation
D. Yes — because Visitor makes adding new node types trivial

CORRECT ANSWER: A. Yes — Visitor suits a stable element set with frequently-added operations, isolating each operation in its own visitor (accepting that new element types would be costly)

RATIONALE:
Visitor fits stable element hierarchies with many added operations; its known cost is that introducing a new element type forces edits to every visitor.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Behavioural module content
* Gamma, Helm, Johnson, Vlissides (1994), Design Patterns

CATEGORY: IDIOMS
MODULE: PIMPL (Pointer to Implementation)
MODULE ID: idioms-pimpl
ASSESSMENT TYPE: Formal Pre-Test — Form A

================================================================Question 1
Question ID: idioms-pimpl:A1
Paired Question ID: idioms-pimpl:B1
Competency ID: pimpl-intent
Bloom Level: Remember
Difficulty: Easy
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
What does the PIMPL (pointer to implementation) idiom do?

OPTIONS:
A. Converts one interface into another
B. Shares intrinsic state across many objects
C. Hides a class’s private members behind a pointer to an implementation struct, keeping the header stable
D. Provides one-to-many notification

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. Hides a class’s private members behind a pointer to an implementation struct, keeping the header stable

RATIONALE:
PIMPL moves private members into a hidden implementation accessed through a pointer, so the header stays stable. The distractors are Adapter, Flyweight, and Observer.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Sutter (1999), Exceptional C++ — Compilation Firewalls (PIMPL)
* CodiNeo Idioms module content

------------------------------------------------
Question 2
Question ID: idioms-pimpl:A2
Paired Question ID: idioms-pimpl:B2
Competency ID: pimpl-classify
Bloom Level: Understand
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
How should PIMPL be classified relative to the GoF design patterns?

OPTIONS:
A. As the GoF Bridge pattern
B. As a GoF structural pattern
C. As a GoF creational pattern
D. As a C++ implementation idiom (a compilation firewall), distinct from Bridge

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. As a C++ implementation idiom (a compilation firewall), distinct from Bridge

RATIONALE:
PIMPL is a C++ implementation idiom for header insulation, not a GoF pattern, and is distinct from Bridge (which varies an abstraction/implementation hierarchy).

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Sutter (1999), Exceptional C++ — Compilation Firewalls (PIMPL)
* Nesteruk (2022), Design Patterns in Modern C++

------------------------------------------------
Question 3
Question ID: idioms-pimpl:A3
Paired Question ID: idioms-pimpl:B3
Competency ID: pimpl-select
Bloom Level: Apply
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A widely-included library header keeps changing its private members, forcing slow recompiles of every client. You want the header to stop exposing those members so clients rarely recompile. Which technique fits?

OPTIONS:
A. The PIMPL idiom (pointer to a hidden implementation)
B. The Composite pattern
C. The Observer pattern
D. The Strategy pattern

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. The PIMPL idiom (pointer to a hidden implementation)

RATIONALE:
Insulating clients from private-member changes via a hidden implementation pointer is PIMPL. The distractors solve unrelated problems.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Sutter (1999), Exceptional C++ — Compilation Firewalls (PIMPL)

------------------------------------------------
Question 4
Question ID: idioms-pimpl:A4
Paired Question ID: idioms-pimpl:B4
Competency ID: pimpl-identify
Bloom Level: Analyze
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
Examine (widget.h):  class Widget { public: Widget(); ~Widget(); void draw(); private: struct Impl; std::unique_ptr<Impl> p; };  with all members defined in widget.cpp. Which C++ idiom does this header demonstrate?

OPTIONS:
A. Bridge pattern
B. PIMPL idiom
C. Flyweight pattern
D. Proxy pattern

CORRECT ANSWER:
B. PIMPL idiom

RATIONALE:
An opaque nested Impl held by a smart pointer, with definitions in the .cpp, is the PIMPL idiom (a compilation firewall).

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Sutter (1999), Exceptional C++ — Compilation Firewalls (PIMPL)
* Nesteruk (2022), Design Patterns in Modern C++

------------------------------------------------
Question 5
Question ID: idioms-pimpl:A5
Paired Question ID: idioms-pimpl:B5
Competency ID: pimpl-evaluate
Bloom Level: Evaluate
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft

QUESTION:
A developer says a class “uses Bridge” because it holds a unique_ptr<Impl>. But there is exactly one Impl type whose only purpose is to hide private members and reduce header dependencies — no abstraction/implementation hierarchy varies. Is “Bridge” accurate?

OPTIONS:
A. Yes — any pointer-to-impl is Bridge
B. Yes — Bridge and PIMPL are interchangeable terms
C. No — a single fixed implementation used only to hide members and stabilise the header is the PIMPL idiom, not Bridge
D. No — this is actually the Adapter pattern

CORRECT ANSWER:
C. No — a single fixed implementation used only to hide members and stabilise the header is the PIMPL idiom, not Bridge

RATIONALE:
With one fixed implementation used solely for header insulation, this is PIMPL, not Bridge — Bridge requires independently varying abstraction and implementation hierarchies.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Sutter (1999), Exceptional C++ — Compilation Firewalls (PIMPL)
* Nesteruk (2022), Design Patterns in Modern C++

QUESTION:
The PIMPL idiom primarily ___.

OPTIONS:
A. shares intrinsic state across objects
B. defines interchangeable algorithms
C. captures and restores object state
D. moves a class’s private implementation behind a pointer so the header stays stable and dependencies shrink

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. moves a class’s private implementation behind a pointer so the header stays stable and dependencies shrink

RATIONALE:
PIMPL relocates the private implementation behind a pointer to insulate the header and cut dependencies. The distractors are Flyweight, Strategy, and Memento.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Sutter (1999), Exceptional C++ — Compilation Firewalls (PIMPL)
* CodiNeo Idioms module content

QUESTION:
Which benefits does PIMPL provide?

OPTIONS:
A. Reduced header/compile-time dependencies, a more stable header/ABI, and hidden implementation details
B. Runtime algorithm swapping
C. Automatic object pooling
D. One-to-many event notification

CORRECT ANSWER:
A. Reduced header/compile-time dependencies, a more stable header/ABI, and hidden implementation details

RATIONALE:
PIMPL’s benefits are fewer header dependencies, a more stable header/ABI, and hidden implementation details. The distractors describe Strategy, Flyweight, and Observer.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Sutter (1999), Exceptional C++ — Compilation Firewalls (PIMPL)

QUESTION:
A class exposes only public methods in its header and forwards every call to a privately-pointed Impl defined in the source file, with one Impl type whose sole job is hiding members. Is calling this “PIMPL” (not Bridge) correct?

OPTIONS:
A. No — it must be called Bridge
B. No — PIMPL requires multiple implementations
C. Yes — but only if it is in the standard library
D. Yes — a single hidden implementation behind a pointer to insulate the header is PIMPL, distinct from Bridge’s varying abstraction/implementation hierarchies

CORRECT ANSWER:
D. Yes — a single hidden implementation behind a pointer to insulate the header is PIMPL, distinct from Bridge’s varying abstraction/implementation hierarchies

RATIONALE:
A single hidden implementation behind a pointer used to insulate the header is PIMPL; Bridge would require independently varying abstraction and implementation hierarchies.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* Sutter (1999), Exceptional C++ — Compilation Firewalls (PIMPL)
* Nesteruk (2022), Design Patterns in Modern C++

QUESTION:
What does the PIMPL idiom move out of a class’s public header?

OPTIONS:
A. Its private members, into a hidden implementation type reached through a pointer
B. Its public methods, into a base class
C. Its constructor, into a factory
D. Its return types, into templates

CORRECT ANSWER: A. Its private members, into a hidden implementation type reached through a pointer

RATIONALE:
PIMPL relocates private members into a hidden Impl type accessed via a pointer, keeping the header stable.

Question 2
Question ID: idioms-pimpl:understanding
Assessment Type: In-Module Conceptual Assessment
Source Question Index: 1
Competency ID: pimpl-why-recompile
Bloom Level: Understanding
Applicable: YES
Difficulty: Moderate
Validation Status: draft
QUESTION FORMAT: FOUR-OPTION MCQ

QUESTION:
Why does PIMPL reduce client recompilation when private members change?

OPTIONS:
A. Because clients stop including any headers
B. Because PIMPL disables the preprocessor
C. Because the compiler caches everything forever
D. Because the private members live in the .cpp, so changing them does not change the header clients include

CORRECT ANSWER: D. Because the private members live in the .cpp, so changing them does not change the header clients include

RATIONALE:
With private state hidden in the implementation file, header-included clients are insulated from those changes and need not recompile.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Idioms module content
* Sutter (1999), Exceptional C++ — Compilation Firewalls (PIMPL)

A. Make the class a Singleton
B. Add more public getters
C. Hide the private members behind a pointer to an implementation defined in the .cpp
D. Convert the class to a template

CORRECT ANSWER: C. Hide the private members behind a pointer to an implementation defined in the .cpp

RATIONALE:
Hiding private members behind an implementation pointer (PIMPL) stabilises the header and cuts client recompiles.

Question 5
Question ID: idioms-pimpl:evaluating
Assessment Type: In-Module Conceptual Assessment
Source Question Index: 4
Competency ID: pimpl-vs-bridge
Bloom Level: Evaluating
Applicable: YES
Difficulty: Difficult
Validation Status: draft
QUESTION FORMAT: FOUR-OPTION MCQ

QUESTION:
A class holds one unique_ptr<Impl> whose sole job is hiding private members and stabilising the header — no abstraction varies. Is calling it “Bridge” accurate?

OPTIONS:
A. Yes — any pointer-to-implementation is Bridge
B. No — this is actually Adapter
C. Yes — Bridge and PIMPL are the same thing
D. No — a single fixed implementation used only to insulate the header is PIMPL; Bridge needs independently varying abstraction and implementation hierarchies

CORRECT ANSWER: D. No — a single fixed implementation used only to insulate the header is PIMPL; Bridge needs independently varying abstraction and implementation hierarchies

RATIONALE:
One fixed Impl for header insulation is PIMPL, not Bridge; Bridge requires an abstraction and an independently varying implementation hierarchy.

SOURCE REFERENCES:
* CodiNeo Idioms module content
* Nesteruk (2022), Design Patterns in Modern C++
